Blackie’s Modern Cyclopedia.
I This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive
publications of its class, it contains an
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume compendium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed ;
the set of eight volumes for 505., packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia,

Dear Bulletin, —Perth has moved ahead
a bit since the year of the Kimberley rush. I
remember, in ’B6, I wanted a glass for my watch,
and called on a watchmaker and jeweller in the
main street, only to find his door closed, and an
envelope gummed on it with the words idn the
jury till 4 o’clock.” I called next day, and then
the legend ran, ‘ 6 Still on that blessed jury.
B.L.W.

S.M. Herald says ; “ If the retrenchment
party succeeds in cutting down large salaries, the
present holders will retire and their places will be
filled by medium men.” This is based on the
assumption that none of the present holders are
medium men, and that they can all hud good
billets to retire to at any moment. Yet when
Berry sacked dozens of the high-salaried Victorian
civil servants, what a number of them retired to
beggary, or suicide, or the lunatic asylum. i here
was no demand for them anywhere. They were
poor, dull fossils who were utterly useless outside
the service and nearly useless in it. At least na
the civil service, if it retired in dudgeon because
its salary was reduced, would straightway start
on that downward road whose end is a disused
tank on a waste allotment.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
An Tip to Date A B C for Young
Australia,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
(Warranted to make any true - born Horsetralian
youngster grasp the mysteries of his alphabet in halt
the usual time.)
A Stands for Arihi, fit, well, and strong,
This year she romped in for the Maribyrnong.
B Stands for Bungebah, son of Grandmaster;
Some are shaped better, but none can go faster.
0 Stands for Carbine, the best horse alive —
Won the coveted Cup with the crusher ten-
five.
D Stands for Devon, from The West by Musk
Bose,
Second in three Cups and beat by a nose.
E Stands for Ebor, by Robert the Devil,
A champion o’er sticks and a don on the level.
F Stands for Forward, Gang Forward his sire—
For the Ninety-four Derby was reckoned a
flyer.
G Stands for Glenloth, who has gone to the stud.
Waltzed off with the Ninety-two Cup in the
mud.
H Stands for Harvester, Sammy Cook’s nag;
He wins all the way from the fall of the flag.
1 Stands for Isaac, who don’t cry “ Old Clothes;”
He both bleeds his backers, and bleeds at the
nose.
J Stands for Jeweller, from old Souvenir,
A great goer once, but his legs now are queer.
K Stands for Killarney, who comes from the
West;
When tackling Timoni he oame off second-
best.
L Stands for Loyalty, by St. George from Fair
Nell;
How he failed all this season the turf-records
tell.
M Stands for Marvel, the son of La Belle;
A champion before whom old Carbine once
fell.
N Stands for Nada, well backed for last Cup,
She was bumped in the straight or she might
have got up.
O Stands for Oxide, his dam Geraldine,
As honest and game as a horse was e’er seen.
P Stands for Paris and Patron ; the pair
Won the double this year, but my “ stuff ”
wasn’t there.
Q Stands for Quiver, “ shot bird ” for the Oaks,
Moore stopped at the barrier, the stewards
stopped his jokes.
R Stands for Ruenalf, son of old Grand Flaneur,
He lost the last Cup, though they reckoned
him sure.
S Stands for Shylock, a crack over fencing,
When the others are flogging he’s only com-
mencing.
T Stands for Tarcoola, his owner, J. Cripps ;
Last year won “ hands down ” with the rest
at the whips.
CJ Stands for Uabba ; the Epsom he won,
With Volney behind him, a son of a gun.
V Stands for Vere, by Newminster-Ouida,
Scobie’s her trainer, and Bailey’s her breeder.
W Stands for Wardance, a well-bred brown horse,
Who’s been badly beaten on many a course.
X Stands for Xenophon, who ran about Bourke
With the name of a Greek and the heart of a
Turk.
Y Stands for Yarran, who ran many a race,
And is well worth a pound for a win or a place,
Z Stands for Zulu, won the eighty-one Cup,
As rank an outsider as ever got up.
WARREGO
Dear Bulletin,-—” Binghi ” is remarkably
correct about bush orgies and fighting; yet, to a
certain extent, he requires correction. Men do not
fight in Australia so much as “at ’ome.” But
they talk fight. By “ they ” I mean the mobsman
or gregarious bushman ; the yearly epidemic, not
the_ endemic rustic; the shearing, harvesting,
racing or cane-cutting lot. I also allude to the
bar-and-verandah-form mob of town loafers, and
they are the pick of the trash. I know a bush-
man, however, one “Slippery,” who will talk fight
as soon as he has one drink in him. But he will
fight. On his behalf I challenged all the fighting
youths to stand up for four rounds in a hall. Where
were they ? The verandah family, who had just
stated that anyone of them could “ do ” Slippery,
heard the challenge and left. The barber’s lot
chuckled buc did not come forward. The long-
beerians, rabbiters, spielers, fat-heads, slanter-
bookies, &0., were all there and as certainly “not
there.” And yet Slippery had nothing to go on.
He can’t fight; he always gets beaten ; he has no
science, no constitution, and won’t train as long as
“ loner-’uns ” are plentiful. But he will fight, and
likes it, and they feared him. The Australian
won’t fight his equal unless in liquor. I have
asked dozens just to stand up and see if my hand
was in. The other side was all blasphemy, brag,
and hurried exit. Again, when I have been chal-
lenged by a man whom I knew (and who knew
himself) to be my superior with fists, I never
could get a trial on with sticks, bricks in a sack,
iron rods, or anything that would break a bone.
One excuse was that ‘‘perhaps I had practised.”
No, we don’t fight in Australia —unless we know
our marks.— Scotty the Wrinkler.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Cornspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable 3IS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for s vine on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e.,'any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.

News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 3s. 3d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 5, 1895.
The House of Forty-Eight Rich
Landlords.
The Victorian Land and Income-Tax Bill
has got through the Assembly and reached
the Upper .House, and there, as anticipated
by everybody who knew the record of that
fatuous Chamber, the progressive policy of the
Turner Government has met with its first
serious check. The immediate action of the
Council, on receiving the obnoxious bill, was
to postpone its consideration for several
weeks, and all the efforts of the Govern-
ment’s representative, Mr. Cuthbbrt, to in-
duce it to go on with business were met with
blank refusal. The Council was in a hostile
and aggressive mood, and was anxious-to
show it as definitely as possible. And when
that august but doddering body does proceed
to seriously consider the measure, it is tole-
rably certain that it will either be altogether
rejected, or will be amended until it loses
all semblance of its original shape and pur-
pose. That much was tolerably certain
many weeks ago, but Sargood’s resignation,
and his open declaration of war against the
Government, and the Council’s aggressive
attitude of a few days ago, all tend to make
it still more certain. Unless under the
influence of absolute terrorism, the Victorian
Upper House will never give its assent to
Premier Turner’s scheme for taxing the big
landowners and capitalists of the province in
order to fill the yawning void in the revenue.
And, when it has rejected the Bill, Victoria
will once more be brought up short against
the problem which it has already met so
often, and has so often failed to solve, and
which it yet must solve sooner or later.
* * *
The Victorian Council is like hardly any-
thing else of its kind on this earth. It is
more distinctly Tory than the British House
of or the Legislative Councils of the
six other provinces of Australasia, and it is
more elaborately secured against popular
pressure than any of them. It was con-
structed with a strange and fiendish in-
genuity which makes it almost impossible for
any man with even a semblance of liberal
sentiment to get into it, and which makes
it an epitome of the aggregate greed,
selfishness, bigotry and hopeless Toryism
of the province. It enjoys the distinction of
being the very worst thing of its species in
Australasia, and pretty nearly the worst
thing of its species in this world. It can’t be
dissolved, neither can it dissolve itself ; the
members are elected for six years, and as
they retire in rotation, the country never
even enjoys the doubtful advantage of getting
a new Council; there are always two-thirds of
the old horror left. It is based mainly on a
property suffrage, the qualification being the
ownership of a freehold valued for rating
purposes at £lO per annum, or of a lease-
hold valued at £25 per annum. The only
persons, not being landholders, who can
vote at Council elections are doctors of
medicine, certificated schoolmasters, matricu-
lated students of Melbourne University,
military and naval officers, parsons, bar-
risters, solicitors, and graduates of British
Universities. Considering the incurable
Toryism of at least seven out of these
nine classes, the Council's constituency
may be set down, for all practical purposes,
as one that embodies all the Conservative
elements of the community and leaves out
all others.
# * *
This, however, is by no means all. There
is a qualification for members of the Council
as well as for electors, and no one can enter
that chamber of solemn decay unless he owns
freehold property to the rateable value of at
least £lOO per annum. Land is the one and
only test of fitness to direct the destinies of
Victoria in the Upper House. A man may
have learning, genius, high character, un-
bounded popularity, and may possess
£10,000,000 in cash or Government stock,
but unless he owns the requisite amount of
land he is, so far as the Victorian Legislative
Council is concerned, but sounding brass and
a tinkling cymbal. The Council is a body
of large landholders elected by landholders
and lawyers. It is the outward and visible
sign of mould, and decayed vegetation, and
grass, and large fat worms, and rotted flesh
and other fertilisers, and it is earthy like
the constituency which it represents.
Therefore on every point where the interests
of the large landholder can possibly come
into collision with those of the people who
do not own land, or where the interests of
the man with a. large income are diametri-
cally opposed to those of the man with a
small one, the Council blocks the way. It
is against land-taxes, income-taxes, and
absentee-taxes ; it is against the withdrawal
of the subsidies paid to local bodies, against
an efficient Betterment Act which would
compel landholders whose property values
were increased by public works to pay part
of the cost thereof, against land nationalisa-
tion, against a State Bank, against one-man-
one-vote, against any attempt to restrict the
growth of big estates, against any effective
scheme of banking-reform—in short, against
nearly everything which the country most
requires. It came into existence at a time
when almost every iniquity which the Fat
Man could desire was embodied in the
statute-book, therefore it did not require to
legislate to secure its ends ; it had merely to
spread itself across the track and prevent
legislation. The role it had to fill was
that of a dead and putrefying mass lying
on the national chest, and all through its
career it has filled this role to perfection.
• • *
In Maoriland, Queensland and New
South Wales the Upper House is a nominee
body. Therefore, a Democratic Ministry
can swamp it with liberal nominees in order
to bring it in line with the popular will,
and, if the people demand its abolition,
can fill it with representatives who are
pledged to pass a bill wiping themselves out
of legislative existence. In South Australia
and Tasmania the Council is elected on a
property qualification, but there is no pro-
perty qualification for Councillors them-
selves, consequently it often happens that
the mass of small property holders support
the Democratic cause as against the Tory
instincts of the big landlords, and return
men of liberal ideas to represent them in
the Chamber of Property. But Victoria
struck the great and wonderful idea of
making only big landlords eligible to sit in the
Upper House, and consequently the interests
of bigotry and class prejudice are better
guarded in that province than in any other.
There are a larger proportion of property-
holders in Victoria than in any other of the
Australian provinces. The property-holder
has the Upper House all to himself, and, by
virtue of his dual vote, he has often a
majority in the Assembly also. When the
Conservative party is uppermost in the
Assembly, the two Houses are unanimous
in helping along the blessed interests of
Property. When the Democratic party is
uppermost, the Council blocks its attempts
at progress and waits peacefully for better
times. Therefore, the odds are always two
to one in favor of the blessed interests of
Property,
* * •
For the Democratic party, the Victorian
Legislative Council is the most complicated
and the most hopeless problem in Australian
politics. Everything else seems to be pos-
sible, but the reform of the Victorian Upper
House by any constitutional means is, to all
appearance, impossible. Yet the fact that it
will be reformed, or abolished, sooner or
later, is sufficiently obvious. Such an
anomaly as this House of 48 rich landlords
cannot possibly go on for ever. And if, as
seems more than probable, the Council
rejects the Land and Income Tax Bill, there
will be an urgent necessity for beginning the
process of reform at once. If that measure
is rejected, then the only apparent alterna-
tives are either to borrow money for revenue
purposes and thus stave off the difficulty
until to-morrow, or to go bankrupt. The
former possibility should not be considered
even for an instant. Under the Munro,
Shiels, and Patterson regimes the country
has already borrowed some three millions
for revenue purposes, in addition to the
many millions previously borrowed for public
works, and if the large landholders of the
Council once more definitely refuse to pay
their share of principal and interest, it is
time to stop borrowing and financing and
striving hopelessly to make ends meet, and
give notice that the province proposes to go
broke, so far as its foreign creditors are con-
cerned, in the most seemly manner that cir-
cumstances will admit. Then Victoria can
remain broke until the Council consents
to pass an Act decreeing its own aboli-
tion, and when that is done, and the
taxation system lias been put on a more
equitable basis, it will be time enough to
resume payment. If the Council remains
obdurate, then the result will be that
the province will remain broke indefinitely.
The natural and logical attitude for the
Assembly to assume is simply this : If the
Council declines to tax itself and its class to
help to pay the public creditor, then it must
efface itself and leave the question of ways
and means entirely to the people’s represen-
tatives, or else the public creditor must slide.
* * *
Hitherto the rich landlords of the Vic-
torian Upper House have travelled on the
assumption that if they refused to tax them-
selves when money was urgently wanted, the
Assembly would lay their share of the bur-
den on the masses rather than allow the
province to default. It has said, in effect, to
the class which the Assembly mainly repre-
sents, that it must make up the deficiency
or else the country must go bankrupt,
and the stupid, dull, patient, ox-like
masses have made up the deficiency
accordingly. But in the present financial
emergency—by far the worst which Victoria
has yet encountered—it is time for the
people’s representatives to present the
Council with its own alternative, and if that
unpatriotic institution adheres to its old
policy then the Assembly will be well-
advised to take it at its word. If the country
defaults in its dealings with its English
creditors through the selfish action of the
Upper House, it will at least arouse the
people of Victoria to a fuller appreciation of
that incubus, and cause them to attempt
some definite effort for its abolition. There
is no legal means by which it can be shifted ;
the only thing which is likely to cause it to
abolish itself is active and universal execra-
tion, and next time the Council deliberately
invites public execration the popular
Chamber, at all events, should do nothing at
all to avert it. What Victoria has to keep
steadily in view is that its House of Bloated
Affluence is almost the worst specimen of its
kind in Christendom; that there is prac-
tically no hope of any important reforms
until it is either obliterated or wholly
altered ; and that it is hardly possible to pay
too high a price for the removal of the
ancient incubus. If the country will only
get and retain a firm hold upon these three
facts, then all the elaborate safeguards of the
Constitution cannot keep the House of the
48 Rich Landlords afloat very much longer.
An Imperial Zollverein,
The Statist (London) offers a prize of £lOOO
for a scheme of Britannic Customs Union. The
Zollverein of Germany governs the trade and
commerce of that extraordinary group of States
on Protectionist principles, under one tariff,
with a success opposed to all economic laws as
exclusively communicated by Nature to the
English and Australian Freetrade press.
Strange indeed it is that the Freetrade Statist
should touch, handle or advertise the “Fair-
trade ” doctrine of a Zollverein for the British
Empire! At present, no doubt, Freetraders are
all aghast in England, and Protectionists all
aglow, while the magazines teem with articles on
what is really the nucleus of a reaction towards
Protection. It will, perhaps, make the matter
clearer to quote the creed of a prominent
English Fairtrader —one Farrer Ecroyd ;
That, in view of the growing injury inflicted upon our
industries by foreign tariffs, and the consequent import-
ance of developing the resources of India and the colo-
nies, it is expedient to free ourselves as early as possible
from the restraints of commercial treaties; to abolish
duties upon tea, coffee, cocoa, and dried fruits imported
from British possessions ; to levy specific duties (in no
case equal to more than 10 per cent, upon ordinary
average values) upon the like articles, as well as upon
wheat, flour, and sugar imported from foreign countries ;
and also to impose an import duty upon foreign manufac-
tures, with the notification that it should cease to operate,
as against each nation, from the day on which such nation
should admit British manufactures duty-free.
The United Kingdom levies duties on tobacco,
tea, spirits, wine, currants, coffee, raisins, and
a few other articles —and the Fairtraders pro-
pose that these duties should be remitted as
against colonies. Consistently enough, the
Cobden Club agrees to this part of the pro-
posal, but objects strenuously to the imposition
of any new duties, either for protection, or re-
taliation, or reciprocity. Governments, it says,
cannot create trade, and they cannot divert it
without diminishing it. And now the Statist
steps in to admit that there is something to be
said against Freetrade. This is the unkindest
cut of all, for the Statist has always hitherto
been a “right-thinking” person.

England’s manufacturing supremacy is being
challenged. By a singular Nemesis, it is Man-
chester that feels the first effects of foreign
competition. Year hy year the total export;
of cotton goods decrease. Here are the figure;;
for a series of years ;—

The “ home competition ” in cotton is, of
course, greater each year, hut the manufacturer
appears to think that it is only a question of
time till he is attacked on that market too.
Indeed, it is now reckoned that the Indian
spinner has, for the Eastern trade, an advantage
of at least six-tenths of a penny per lb. over his
Lancashire rival. India increased her cotton
exports to China, Hongkong and Japan from
8 million pounds in ’76 to 115 million pounds
in ’90, in the face of British opposition. If
India thus quickly builds up a trade —and she
has only attempted the coarser textures
so far—what must be feared when other
countries seriously enter the contest? How
important it therefore becomes to Man-
chester that “ the colonial market ” should
be secured against non-British competition !
Despite falling prices, the sterling value of
important manufactured imports into Great
Britain and Ireland shows an increase. Thus
silk manufactures totalled ,£10,269,000 in
1885 and £11,289,000 in 1892. The figures for
woollen manufactures and iron manufactures
have the same aspect :

The impression is gaining ground that the
success of the United Kingdom has been in
many respects due to luck. The invention of
railways by Stephenson was not to be credited
to any fiscal excellence —yet when the world
wanted railways it was England’s lot to have
facilities of supply. Stephenson did a part
and iron and.coal in proximity to the sea-board
did the rest. It was no excellence of England’s
tariff that found Germany divided against her-
self till 16th April, 1871 ; that gave France in-
ternal war through the century and the Franco-
German war and the cruel indemnity to cap it
all; that sent the Civil War to the United
States. England has benefited in the past by
her neighbours’ misfortunes. Their extremity
was her opportunity. In the past she has had
success ; it almost looks now as if she were
going to deserve it. But it must be remem-
bered that the only Zollverein Australia wants
is one of her own provinces.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The logic of Shoddy
Sydney has an incorrigible Free-raider named
E. Pulsford, who is never tired of showing
how Australia may gain the whole world ami
lose her own soul after the mediod of Man-
chester. It never seems to occur to Manchester
missionaries that people may have other aims
in life than trying how to best their neighbours
at a bargain, and build a whited sepulchre of
commercial success full of dead ideals and Mam-
mon’s uncleanness. The hero of Manchester is
the man who jews and screws and scrapes until
he has accumulated a large bank-balance and a
dirty little soul the size of sixpence. Man-
chester’s abomination is the man who doesn’t
make a “ success in life” because he piles up
thoughts and deeds instead of dollars. And
the Pulsfords and M’Millans, the Robert
Reids and Sargoods take for granted that Aus-
tralia is ready to purchase “ trade” at any price
of sordid degradation and national wretched-
ness. The Freetrade game at present is to get
the provinces to lake advantage of the very
liberal clause in the recent British-Japanese
treaty, by which Australian exports are made
welcome in Japan on the trifling condition that
unlimited Japanese are made welcome in
Australia. So E. Pulsford, Esq., takes
a column and a quarter of Sydney Tele-
graph to prove that the exclusion of
Chinese from N.S. Wales by the poll-tax has
not really benefited the province, and that the
sooner poll-taxes are abolished the sooner Aus-
tralian trade with China and Japan will begin
to boom. And, in order to show this, he prints
a table which declares that, in the 11 years,
1878-88, 29,310 Chinese came into N.S. Wales,
while in the five subsequent years, 1889-93, only
94 arrived. In 1886, 3092 came; in 1887, 4436;
in 1888, 1848. Then the poll-tax was clapped
on, and in ’B9, only 7 sad Chinamen came, while
941 went away. In the five years, 1884-88, the
surplus of arrivals over departures was 5514.
In the five years, 1889-93, the surplus of depar-
tures over arrivals was 3378. From which E.
Pulsford draws the astonishing deduction that
the Exclusion Act of ’BB has acted rather as an
Inclusion Act ! This is the result of a cotton
diet on the intellect. E. Pulsford then, in
genuine Manchester fashion, pi’oceeds to point
out how much profit the steamboat companies
lost when their traffic in Chinese was knocked
on the head. Every Free-traitor would be
proud and happy to see Australian workmen
starved out of existence if only “ the steam-
boat companies ” (all run with English money)
made enormous profits. The Chinaman is
indeed the glorious exemplar of the creed
that man was made for £ s. d. He can
work 16 hours a day on a handful of rice,
and he has no ideals, no aspirations. But he
always “ gets on,” and is content to live a dog’s
life and die a dog’s death for the sake of
“getting on.” That’s the man—or rather,
that’s the beast—for the Foreign-traders. But E.
Pulsfohd’s final argument is unworthy of Man-
chester. It seems that Australia owes a debt
of gratitude to China for absorbing silver and
keeping up the price of Broken Hill stock
(which isn’t kept up, by the way), therefore we
should do our utmost to repay the debt by
“not excluding from our shores one large
section of the people who are taking our
silver.” It doesn’t seem to strike this calico


Wesbralia’s coat of arms—a tin-opener and
waterbag.
logician that if the Chinese in China are the
people who take our silver, then, on his own
showing, the more Chinese we bring to Aus-
tralia the fewer there will be left at home to
use that same silver, and, therefore, Broken
Hill stock will fall faster than ever. Nor does
E. Pulsford bother to think that it obviously
pays us better to keep the Chinese at a distance
as customers for our silver than to bring them
close as competitors for our gold. And, lastly,
the argument is a fool of an argument, con-
sidering that whatever silver China does take
from Australia she takes simply because she
wants it, and if she didn’t want it she wouldn’t
take it, and there is no question of gratitude
about the matter. The idea that the man who
sells E. Pulsford a pair of boots should be so
overwhelmingly thankful as to assist him to
open a bootshop next door, is not in accordance
with Manchester maxims, and should be
seriously reconsidered by the little Mogul of
the New South Wales foreign-trade home-slave
party.
Codlin, not Short!
When, in Sept, last, the Vic. elections resulted
so disastrously for the Conservatives, The Bul-
letin drew attention to a letter in the Argus,
vhich disclaimed the title of “Conservative”
as applied in Australia. As then predicted, the
Argus has since continued officially to hint its
desire to be classed as Liberal and Democratic.
Here is a touch of this in reference to the Vic.
Legislative Council:—
The less representative the body the less its moral and
intellectual claim to impose or remit taxation, and after
all it is upon the moral and intellectual claim that these
cases must rest, and not upon the obscure wording of dry-
as-dust statutes, framed by men who did not live in a
democratic generation, and who were not in touch with
democratic ideas.
The claim that the Argus is in touch with
democratic ideas is more courageous than
creditable. The ultimate test of a Conserva-
tive, as of a Democrat, is the Land Question.
Fiscal issues may become mere matters of
expediency, and the form of government may
be settled beyond dispute—but the Land Ques-
tion will not be buried in many a land for
many a day. Whether the community shall
own the land, or the land shall own
the community this is the question
written on the chief line of demarcation
between Democrat and Conservative. The
Democrat decides in favor of the Community
and the Conservative in favor of the Land. In
Victoria, the Argus is found contesting for the
enlargement of the powers of the Council, so
that it shall be co-equal with the Assembly in
matters of taxation. The Vic. Council consists
of property-owners elected by property-owners.
Victoria is the only province of the group that
insists on the property qualification for repre-
sentatives— the amount being fixed at a
minimum of £lOO a year. The Argus
fatuously attempts the juggling trick of
supporting simultaneously a Fat Man
House and its own conceit that it is
a Democratic journal. It disingenuously
instances the practice in South Australia with
regard to money bills, the Council there being
allowed to make “suggestions” to the Assembly
—but S.A. Ms. L.C. receive £2OO a year and
need no property-qualification! It undoubtedly
is anomalous in a democracy to have a bi-
cameral system with one of the Chambers of
imperfect powers—but in the present case it is
not the democracy but the Chamber that is
anomalous, and the sooner it is put on a
democratic basis the better. Meanwhile, the
Argus will do least harm to its real cause by
maintaining a judicious silence about the
blubbery anachronism that does duty for
Victoria’s Second House.
The Failure of Democracy.
Annie Besant is said to be disgusted with the
failure of democracy in Australia, “ and it is
likely that her opinioms»will be expressed in no
hesitating fashion in some of the English
magazines.” A good many other people are
likewise disgusted with the failure of democracy
in Australia, and in many other places as well.
Few of these, however, stop to consider that
monarchy and aristocracy have been failures for
5000 years, or thereabouts, while democracy
only began to be seriously heard of on any large
scale half-a-century ago, and isn’t really
started|yet. When half-a-dozen large, civilised
nations have run an absolutely democratic
Government—a Government where the whole
Legislature, the President, and all the other
powers that be, are elected by adult suffrage
only—for 1000 years, then it will be possible to
decide whether democracy is a failure or
not. The professed democrats who propose to
throw up their faith in disgust because a
few small, obscure states, with a political
system which is one-third democracy and two-
thirds oligarchy, haven’t revolutionised the
world in a single generation, and who therefore
desire to go back to the system which deluged
the earth with blood for fifty centuries, are not
likely to do much good to any cause. They
belong to the class that would give a two-grain
quinine pill to a Methusaleh in the last stage
of leprosy, and demand that he should become
young and healthy again in one act. The
world is very, very old, and diseased, and
rotten, and unwieldy, and ignorant; and it is
debased by countless ages of oppression, and
weighed down by the ghosts and fetishes and
wandering spooks of rnoreold superstitions than
any man can count. It has first to learn by a
long, slow process of evolution what liberty
really is. Then it has to acquire its liberty
and to defend it when acquired. Then it has
to learn how to use it, and if these
processes show any definite sign of ac-
complishment in another ten centuries the
good work will be making reasonable progress.
It has taken monarchical Europe 1400 years,
dating from the time when Europe as a whole
began to have a written history, to acquire the
first faint glimmer of a knowledge that peace i;
better than war, and it has made sucli linio
progress with its lesson that the bloated arm i
merits of to-day are the biggest and most costly
ever known or heard of. Even the Schey and
G. D. Clark type of democracy moves faster
and to better purpose than that.
The N.S.W. Loan Estimates.
The New South Wales loan estimates, put
forward by the Reid Cabinet, are only one
more evidence that one Government, under the
party system, is very like another, and that
every Government very much resembles all the
rest. Each one starts by pointing out the
infamous manner in which its predecessor pro-
duced bogus surpluses by payingcurrent expenses
out of loan moneys, instead of charging them to
revenue, and then goes and does likewise. By-
and-bye, its turn comes to be thrown out of
office with ignominy, and then it is exposed in
its turn, and its successor also goes and
does likewise. And each Government, when
it is found out, defends itself by pointing
out that those who went before it did exactly
the same thing, which defence squares very
badly with the fact that each Government
starts by denouncing its predecessor for doing
that same thing. The present batch of loan
estimates is no better and not much worse
than the average. It includes £74,000 (in
addition to £200,000 already expended) for
dredging and other works at a certain dirty
little creek which flow's into Cook’s River,
which works seem to have no object that
any one can understand. Then there is
an item of £300,000 for dredging and
other work on various rivers; £50,000 for
“thinning out State forests;” fifty or sixty
small items about sew'ers, court-houses, police-
offices, alterations to gaols, repairs and
alterations to a dredge, and the like ;
fragmentary amounts for little, patchy fortifi-
cations, law expenses in connection wdth land
resumptions, supply of water-meters, and other
trifles, one lunatic asylum, and a number of
sundries. Not one of these items even pro-
fesses to be revenue-producing, and a great
many of them are not even permanent public
works. The dredged rivers will fill up again
and want more dredging; the State forests
won’t keep thinned out; the works at the creek
at Cook’s River, as the Attorney-General him-
self cheerfully admits, are so utterly useless
that the sovereigns might as well be thrown
into Sydney harbour; and the law expenses are
not public works at all, even in the widest
sense of the term. The only possible excuse—
and even that is a bad one—which the country
can show for borrowing money, is that it
is to be spent in revenue-producing under-
takings, and hardly one single work in this year’s
category will produce a single copper. Many of
the items are entirely foolish and unjustifiable,
and the balance should have been constructed en-
tirely out of current revenue. If N.S. Wales
only preserves a semblance of solvency now—-
when her revenue is augmented by £2,200,000
per annum derived from the sale of public
lands—by charging nearly £1,000,000 of current
expenditure to loans, her position some time in
the future, when she can raise no more loans
and her land revenues are practically ex-
hausted, will be an interesting study. The
Reid Government, like all its predecessors,
lives on the good old theory that things will last
its time. What may happen in the time of
those who come after it is a matter of no present
importance.
The ignominious back-down of the New South
Wales Council in its recent squabble with the
Assembly is only one more proof of the elabor-
ate uselessness of that pompous institution.
The Council struck out of the Estimates an
item of £216,000, professedly because it felt that
it was its duty to do so, and from motives of
the purest and most high-souled patriotism.
Then, being remonstrated with by the Govern-
ment’s representative, and lectured on the ill-
judged character of its proceedings, it caved-in
and restored the item. No effort whatever was
made to demonstrate that the expenditure of
the £216,000 was wise or justifiable ; the moral
aspect of the case, whatever it might be, was
the same at the end of the trouble as it was at
the beginning. Therefore, the Council, by its
own showing, either wilfully did what it knew
to be wrong and only repented under pressure,
or else it did what it believed to be rig!it and
hadn’t sufficient moral stamina to keep on
doing it. In any case the nominee Upper
House hasn’t the courage of its opinions,
whether these opinions are right or wrong, and,
under such circumstances, the reasons for the
existence of a nominee Upper House are not
apparent. A legislative body which professes
to know what it ought to do, "yet dare not do it,
is of no use to anybody.
For six years in succession Maoriland’s exports
have been, on the average, £3,000,000 in excess
of her imports, the total excess in the six years
being more than £18,000,000. And yet the
province has paid off none of the principal of
her public and private debts in this period, and
has even failed to pay the whole of the interest;
in other words, Maorilaud has been running
further into debt. When the exports for the
year are £3,330,000 in excess of the imports,
then she is just keeping the account square
with her outside creditors ; when the excess is
less than that she is getting deeper in the mire;
when it is more then she is paying off a fraction
of her vast liabilities. In like manner New
South Wales needs to export every year
£5,000,000 worth more than she imports to
meet the interest on her liabilities out of re-
venue ; Victoria needs to show an excess of
£3,400,000, and Queensland of £3,000,000 per
annum. All this has to be done without any
further borrowing or any more “influx of
foreign capital.” In 1892, which was her best
year for a very long time back, N.S. Wales
only went about £5,000,000 to the bad, and
Victoria about £3,000,000, while Queensland
actually reduced her outside liabilities by over
a million. Altogether Australasian finance can
best be depicted by a very small man trying to
shove a very big boulder uphill. He loses an
inch or two all the time while he is doing his
best, and when he eases off the strain for a
moment he loses a yard or two, or from that up
to a mile and a half, before he knows where he
is. Then he desperately buckles to again, and
when he only loses a foot or two he calls it
“satisfactory progress.’’
Rev. W. S. Fkackelton, a Sydney Presby-
terian clergyman, has come to the Conclusion
that Professor Drummond’s book, “ The Ascent
of Man,” much boomed in sciolistic circles, “has
not advanced knowledge one single whit.” It
is a pity that the average religious mind has
not the capacity for Frackeltonian criticism.
From a scientific point of view, Drummond is
merely a purveyor of flapdoodle to feed dog-
matic fools on. His thoughts are all plagiarised,
and his rhetoric merely obscures the truth
which they may have originally conveyed.
But, to the anxious pietist, dimly realising that
religious myths are intellectually “impossible,”
Drummond is welcome because he gives science a
teleological twist and revises creeds to suit the
times. “ The Ascent of Man,” as an unblush-
ing theft, surpasses even “ Natural Law,” and
Mrs. Lynn Linton’s exposure, in a recent Fort-
nightly, of the writer’s amazing impudence
does him rather less than justice. “ The
Ascent ” is merely a fine-language redaction of
scraps of other people’s learning—a boiling-
down of 50 other men’s brains with Drum-
mond’s name tagged to the lot. But the con-
cl ision that a little brass god is perched on top
of the universe is eminently satisfactory to the
friends of Jonah and Co. ; and so, while Spen-
cer’s books don’t sell, stolen Spencer, garish
witli literary fireworks, has enormous vogue.
Spencer is the prophet, and Drummond pockets
the profit.
Now that Brassey has definitely consented to
come forth and “govern” Victoria, it is high
time to settle the vexed salary-question. There
is a weak disposition in Vic. Assembly, and a
rabid desire in the Council, to let Hopetoun’s
successor be engaged “at home” on the old
<£lo,ooo basis, whereby the Government will be
“ in honor bound” to abandon all idea of vice-
regal retrenchment until Brassey has served
his term. It is an awful fact that Vie. Legis-
lature derives a sneaking sense of satisfaction
from this particular waste of public money.
An overpaid Governor serves as an apology for
the sinful extravagance of their Parliament
buildings. That disreputable edifice costs the
country some ,£30,000 or £40,000 a year in mere
interest on capital, yet nobody, thus far, has
suggested a practical scheme for relieving the
country of a ridiculous burden. The ’Ouse,
with its valuable site, would sell for a good
deal of money. It might be converted into
wool-stores, or insurance-offices, or something,
and a galvanised-iron structure, run up cheap on
the reclaimed West Melbourne swamp, would
suit the Senate admirably. The spectacle of
paid legislators lounging into premises worth a
million sterling to discuss a huge national
deficit is Gilbertian in its absurdity.
A book written by Mr. James Hole and just
published in England on the evils of private
railway - ownership, contains a lot of things
which the Australian should think over very
hard. The following are a few of the argu-
ments advanced for State ownership :
Free competition is impo-sible in railways, and must
give way to monopoly, and monopolies should always be
owned and controlled by the Government in the interest
of the people. At the present moment two-thirds of the
entire mileage of English railways are in the possession
of six companies. Out of the 6000 railway-stations in the
United Kingdom, 4600 are in the hands of a monopoly,
the companies charging what they please for passengers
and freight within the limits prescribed by the acts of
Parliament. In the United States there is the same ten-
dency to monopoly. The Pennsylvania railway, which
has a length of 8000 miles is made up out of 70 smaller
lines. Three years ago 2450 minor lines were absorbed
into 33 large companies, which cover 77,000 miles, and
altogether 50,000 miles belong to 75 companies. Thise
monopolies are not satisfactory to the shareholders them-
selves, and it is asserted on good authority that the profit
on American railway investments is steadily but not
slowly diminishing year by year. Of the stock nearly
two-thirds paid no div. whatever, while more than 18 per
cent, even of the bonds were in default. One-fifth of the
American railways are now in the hands of receivers,
while the conflicts of the great railway managers and
speculators have wrought wholesale ruin and destruc-
tion throughout the republic. There are many objec-
tions to private ownership. The railways of the
United States are estimated to spend nearly £3,000,000 per
annum on legal expenses, and there are the lobby agents,
the traffic associations and costly offices, which areisli-
mated to cost nearly ±32,000,000 a ye r in unnecessary
expenditure. In England there is more public cont rol,
and consequently less injurious results, and yet the waste
is very large. The directors, according to the Parlia-
mentary Joint Committee, are far removed from the
influence of the shareholders, and the latter are to a great
extent a fluctuating, ignorant and helpless body. One
authority asserts that many of the directorates are
crowded with titled directors who know’nothing of busi-
ness life and still less of the democratic requirements and
necessities of the present day; and the history of
English railway legislation is one long struggle to
rectify the error made in giving such extensile powers to
private companies. In addition to the-waste caused by
private ownership, are the excessive charges for freight
and the high fares. It is possible to travel all over the
State railways of Belgium for a little more than a
sovereign, and from Salzburg, through Vienna, Prts-
burg, Buda Pesth to Fiume, a distance of 750 miles,
second class, for a guinea. The old notion thatjeompe-
tition wmuld bring down fares and rates has proved to be
quite erroneous. Sir R. Moore, in reply to a deputation
from Staffordshire, remarked :—“ When the London and
North-western was the only railway to Liverpool we
carried your iron at 25 per cent, less ; now you have three
railways and must pay for them.”
The Australian Star, in. prostration before
its Dibbs fetish, acknowledges the New South
Wales Legislative Council thirteen who dared
a deadlock as its Great High Priest :
Happily, the Legislative Council has a great regard for
constitutional principle, and very properly it made a
decided stand against the immoral proceeding of the
Government and the Assembly. Under the circumstances
it did its duty On several occasions during the
past few years it has been forced because of the craven
conduct of the Assembly to take a determined stand in
the interests of the people, and this last stand is the
noblest of all Nine-tenths of the really sensible
voters in the country have confidence in the Council.
Nine-tenths of the voters whom the Star would
call “really sensible” have confidence in wild-
cat banks, in pharisaic swindlers, in snuffle, in
praying for rain, in the Star, and in them-
selves. That fact proves nothing. The Star’s
attitude is the most hideous illustration yet
afforded in New South Wales of the plumbless
depth of abasement to which Party Government
can hurl a devotee. If Beelzebub backed up
Dibbs against Reid, and the Council stood by
Beelzebub, the Star would find it necessary to
champion the Council. The miraculous con-
ception that the House of Fossils has “a great
regard for constitutional principle,” or can
“ take a determined stand in the interests of
the people,” or in any interests but those in
which its members are individually concerned,
is a proof not to be paralleled of the deadly
hypnotism of the Evil Eye of Government by
Contradiction.

A CLERK’S TEMPTATION.
A bank-clkrk was lately summoned to the
manager’s office to receive instructions regarding
X's overdraft. The principal, after his wont, was
smoking one of those fragrant weeds that make a
man happy and render the bystanders envious.
The poor clerk inhaled the lovely, mild aroma and
computed in hip own mind how long it would take
him until he could sit in the same chair and smoke
a similar cigar. Suddenly he saw a yellow card-
board packet on the desk and recognised Erossard's
Cavour cigars. His joy was great, for he knew
that on leaving his office he could go to any
tobacconist, get for one shilling a packet of
eight Cavours and Enjoy himself as well as his
chief. Erossard’s Cavours are smoked by the
millions ; they are a genuine, democratic article
originating from a republican country at a price
everyone can afford to pay, and make life worth
living.

♦
The resurrection, for the severalth time
this year, of “ Charley’s Aunt ” at Sydney
Criterion is progressing rather badly. There are
the rudiments of an audience every night, but not
much more. Much competition, and the fact that
even Charley’s Brazilian relative can’t bear being
revived too often in a short space of time, are
partly responsible for the trouble, and the rest of
it is probably owing to the fact that Mr. G. A.
Seager, though good u many respects, is by no
means a perfect substitute for Thornton. He
doesn’t look half so humorous as that departed
idol, and a large naked spot in the back of his
head, which he doesn’t even attempt to conceal,
oasts dl-credit on his alleged youth, and makes
the fact that he is a giddy Oxford student look
improbable. Seager doesn’t do Thornton’s busi-
ness so well as Thornton did, and he has no new
business of his own worth speaking of. Also, he
is a sk i-chy and somewhat indefinite-looking
person, v mle Thornton was clear-cut and em-
phatic. As a natural result, the aunt doesn’t
loom quite so large as she did, and the comedy
suffers in consequence. The rest of the co. hasn’t
altered to any dreadful extent. Time has left no
fresh blobs on the countenance of the bloated
solicitor, who is, as before, one of the best
characters in the tale, and pretty Blanche Carlyle
has improved considerably in various ways till her
small part has become a thing of some note. The
days of “Charley’s Aunt,” in the present instance,
will probably be few. As already mentioned, it
has been revived too much for these times of
retrenchment.
* * * *
In the brilliantly-staged “ Cinderella ” at
the Lyceum signs of improvement are manifest,
especially about the leading lady, Miss Ray
J ones. Last week The Bulletin remarked con-
cerning Miss Jones that she was a nice little lady
who sang rather nicely, and it says the same thing
again this week, only with a shade more emphasis.
Also it repeats its commendation of the humorous
stories told by the friend of Mr. Maeintyre, and'
records the fact that the death of Cock Robin has
taken a firm hold on the public, and especially on
the juvenile mind.
* * * *
“The Span of Life,” with which Bland
Holt opened his season at Sydney Royal, is one
of the few melodramas which The Bulletin
regards with cordial approval. There is no
especial reason why it should be called “ The Span
of Life.” Any other name would do as well—in
fact, in almost every melodrama any other name
would do as well; but probably the author con-
sidered that if he called it “ Ham and Eggs ” it
would be less poetic. The new production is one
of the best spectacles on record, and also one of
the best managed. It contains a lighthouse ; a
huge ocean steamer which seems to come straight
at the audience from out of the scenery at the
back of the stage, and just missing destruction by
a hair’s-breadth—not an unusually thick hair
but only the ordinary size—goes away slowly
into the wings; an Arab invasion garnished
by horses and live camels and trimmed
with loud whoops ; an awful chasm across
which three men make a bridge by hanging
on to each other’s feet, while the heroine rushes
across their prostrate forms and escapes her pur-
suers; and many other effects. The steamer is
the best-managed and most realistic thing of its
kind which The Bulletin has yet seen, and the
outbreak of Arabs on camels is a first-class spec-
tacle. B. Holt’s drama also contains about the
most whole-souled and bowelless miscreant going
—a giddy youth who sinks rotten ships for the
sake of the insurance-money, and murders his
brother, and robs his dying mother, and deals in
slaves on the African coast, and otherwise mis-
conducts himself. This person, who is feebly
represented by Mr. Cosgrove, also invents a new
form of homicide, which is almost beyond detec-
tion, and which should be very useful to small
families and schools.
» • •
Mr. Baker, as the good man of the drama,
is at his best, and Baker at his best is about the
finest actor that Australia has produced in our time.
He doesn’t snuffle and look holy in times of pros-
perity, and he doesn’t wail in times of adversity,
and, altogether, he makes the oppressively good
character look real, and healthy, and natural.
Miss Hilda Spong plays well up to his level, and
Mrs. Bland Holt and Bland himself are up to
their usual high standard. Other people and
things deserving favorable comment are Charles
Brown, Nutty Brown, and Miss Flora Anstead (as
a murderous old lady, in a yellow dress), the
scenery, the Arabs, the Leonard family of
acrobats, the troubled sea, the desert, two clever
children named Smith, and other sundries. The
“ Span of Life ” is a con plete and artistic pro-
duction all through, and, as it doesn’t upset
probability any more than is absolutely necessary,
it should have a good future before it.
* * * *
“ The House that Jack Built ” is as gay as
ever, and is joyfully reconstructed at Her
Majesty’s every night, in the presence of a
respectable audience. This panto, might, perhaps,
be made a little more up-to-date if Jack put a
mortgage on the house and was sold up by the
liquidator in the last act; bub we can’t have
everything in this world, and, even in its present
state, the piece is a pleasant affair that goes on its
irrelevant way amid mirth, and red fire, and bril-
liant colorings, and arrives at its irrelevant con-
clusion in a manner that is eminently satisfactory
to all parties concerned.
** * *
The best of all the Xmas shows was the
much-advertised Flying Machine at Chowder Bay
(Sydney). All the “push” and his “donah”
gathered there on Boxing Day, to the number, it
is alleged, of 12,000, and sweltered and drank beer
through the long, hot afternoon, waiting for the
flying of the _ implement which never flew. All
day the machine stood on its tramway getting up
steam and preparing in a dull and hopeless fashion
for the promised performance. It was a humble-
looking thing, cigar-shaped, painted a dull red, and
with canvas wings about 50ft. across. There was
a small boiler in the centre of it, bub no visible
machinery to connect the alleged steam-power with
the pinions, and the whole concern looked about as
active as akedge-anchor. Theenergeticmanagement
kindled a fire in the bowels of the machine early
in the day, and for hours a great column of smoke
went up from it, and a great crowd surged round
and “ poked borak ” at the business manager, and
the treasurer, and the secretary, and the board of
directors. The machine was tied to a tree a good
part of the time to prevent it breaking away pre-
maturely into space. The business-manager went
round with a worried expression like a man who
had much on his mind, and smiled a joyless smile
as the crowd urged him to “ let her fly.” As the
afternoon went on it somehow became generally
understood that if the machine didn’t do some-
thing the manager would be badly “ stoushed,”
and the excitement grew tremendous. At last
about 4 p.m., when the bay was full of
steamers, sailing-boats and skiffs, and the
crowd on shore was thickest and most
profane, the rope was let go, and the Flying
Machine stumbled heavily down its tramway and
fell over, with a loud noise, on to the beach.
There it promptly took fire and the show was
over. A chest of drawers or a dead goat would
have fallen quite as gracefully, and no more
decisively. For a few seconds the assembled push
had a vague idea of rolling the management down
also, but, thinking better of it, contented itself
with rushing down to the beach and cheering the
combusted machine, and kicking the ruins of it.
Then, after one or two irrelevant free-fights, the
show terminated, and the problem of serial navi-
gation remains unsolved. It is vaguely estimated
that 8000 long-beers were consumed during the
experiment, which goes to prove that the vexed
question can’t be settled by beer, anyhow.
However, of the panto, at Melb. Princess’s
and everywhere else, it can be stated, as a weird
fact, that the remarks which fairly apply to them
one week may have very little bearing upon the
performance of a week later. “ Beauty and the
Beast ” got by no means the best start on record
for aW. and M. show. It has improved its form
wonderfully since then, and is doubtless in eager
search after any novelty likely to aggravate
Xmas merriment.
** * *
The Melbourne Royal panto, is some
degrees less incoherent than its rival up the
street. Also, “ Aladdin ”is a more profusely-
topicalised subject, and it contains a larger
amount of corporal punishment on the seat of
“ wholesome fun.” Likewise, Maggie Moore is
the youth who buys the Emperor of China’s
daughter with a bagful of strange rubies, a cir-
cumstance that guarantees an atmosphere of good
humor in the house. A lack of grotesque dancing
and a scarcity of songs with catchy me ! "dies were
urged against “Aladdin” on Boxing Night, but
this latter defect may loom smaller when some of
the ditties that are furnished with lugubrious
tunes have been cut short or abandoned alto-
gether. Considered as a large and thickly-popu-
lated entertainment, at reduced prices, it seems
bound to prosper. A display of juvenile talent
early in the first act includes at least two or three
lively items, and is a distinct improvement upon
the dark traditional opening scene, where the
wicked gnome rumbles unnecessary remarks
through a bloated mask to a mob of prehistoric
insects. The irrelevant Spirit of Delirium
Tremens has been wisely dispensed with.
* # *
The working staff of the panto, com-
prises (besides Miss Maggie Aladdin) H. R.
Roberts as Widow Twankey, Miss Laura Roberts,
a padded edition of Oily Deering in the royal
dressing-gown of a Chinese emperor, several other
people, and a battalion of supernumeraries. Pro-
cessions are tolerably long and frequent, and the
light of Chinese lanterns is shed upon sundry
feasts at which refreshments are not provided. A
few ballets acquit themselves creditably, but the
well-matured brand of bounder, who has brought
up 16 offspring, and buried most of them in the
cold cemetery, appears no more. The grand-
children are (jetting a chance this year,
* * *
The Judge,” produced at Melb. Bijou on
Boxing Night, will not sentence himself to more
than 14 days, if he doesn’t get off with a week.
Improbability exceeds the limit of humor in this
farcical comedy. The germ of human interest is
not there. No respectable middle-class under-
standing can accept a plot that radiates from a
wax baby boy, who gets the wind on his small
stomach and is heard crying in the next room.
The spurious yell of a counterfeit infant plays a
leading part in “ The Judge,” and its character is
quite as real as any printed on the bill. Elton,
Shine, Geo. Ireland, Diver, Miss Clara Thomp-
son, Miss Katherine Hardy, and Miss Nannie
Harding gave valuable support, the ladies scoring
heavily when the baby is present. As for Elton,
he gets captured by a reckless married lady whilst
in the act of bathing his feet, and for 90 minutes,
or thereabouts, he does little beyond doddering to
and fro his bedroom in charge of a feeding-bottle.

Event ’’ after Monday. On New Year’s night he
was to set up “The Nightmare,’’ a farcical

management should supply every visitor to the
dress-circle with a mince-pie, slack backed. The

** * *
A pressman used to tell a luscious story of
how (unknown, you may be sure, to the manage-
ment) he and some friends interviewed the airy
fairy Bartho in her dressing-room at Melbourne
Princess’s, during the Blue Ballet period. Bartho
didn’t know a word of English,, but she was
anxious to make herself agreeable to the visitor,
and promptly fell in with a suggestion 'that
she should display her wonderful maiden .vigor
in a little tug-of-war. The shapely danseuse
caught hold of the pressman’s hands, planted her
kid shoes firmly against his boots, and then the
two young people started to pull. It is possible
that Bartho could have won the contest easily,
but, instead of suddenly landing the strange gen-
tleman upon her powdered shoulder, she elected
to fall forward against his chest with a bashful
“bposhter,” and he was declared the victor.
Tears come into the eyes of that favored journal-
ist when he relates the incident, and dilates upon
the blissfulness of the moment when Bartho
collided with his shirt-front.

indeed. On Boxing Night a scratch co. sup-
ported Miss Ada Rochefort, at the Opera House,

Adelaide has been without a theatrical co.
since Charles Arnold left; even at Xmas, the only
bill of fare was that of a second-rate dramatic co.
at the Royal. Hudson’s Bijou was closed ! The
holy city simply gasps for a good manager, with
money and a small theatre.

Marie Luella, principal hoy in the Melb. Princess panto-
mime', is divinely tall and {i.e., her wig is) divinely fair.
She has a perfect figure ; limbs that delicately taper from
an elegant and substantial circumference down to the
shapely penultimate thinness of a pencil’s point; and a
poise of the head as graceful as a swan’s. Her fault is a
way of biting her words as though afraid to let them come
out of her mouth whole. But the Firm has to be con-
gratulated on Marie Luella.
No Melb. Panto, “boy” of late years has approached
Marie Luella in sheer sweetness —perhaps not even
Billee Barlow. The face of Marie recalls Lelia Roze a
little, but her voice is resonant and strong,
Topsy Hill is the name of a budding Vio. contralto,
from whom great things are expected. She has been
brought up at Lilydale, Ihe picturesque village where
Melba’s childhood was spent, and where David Mitchell
still reigns in a palace of lime and cement.

There can he now no doubt as to the financial success
of “ John-5,-Dreams,” says The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent. The advance booking is said to be pheno-
menal. Haddon Chambers must be piling up money, for
“ The Fatal Card ” is also drawing enormous houses.
Clement Scott has an innuendo respecting the simi-
larity of “The Masqueraders” and “John-i-Dreams,”
inasmuch as in both plays there are two women and a
dreamy man. Chambers has disposed of this by telling
that long before H. A. Jones hatched “The Mas-
queraders,” he (Chambers) had shown him the sketch of
the plot of “ John-a-Dreams ” (which he then wished to
call “Two Women and a Man ”), so that H.A. clearly
lifted the idea from his friend.
Haddon Chambers has been accused of plagiarism in
drawing the character of Kate Cloud, which, it is said,
was taken from Paula Tanqueray. It has now been
shown, writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
that “John-5, Dreams” was in part written long before
the “ Second Mrs. Tanqueray ” was produced or heard of,
and Chambers, so far from being a literary larcenist, has
been the victim of “ the long arm of coincidence.” The
more one sees the play the better one likes it. It is far
and away beyond anything Haddon has done bifore, and
the public verdict is that it is also far and away beyond
anything done by most of the living dramatists. But it
wants a lot of acting, and will no doubt suffer from this
cause when it comes to be produced in Australia, for you
don’t get a Tree or a Mrs. Pat. Campbell, nor a Cartwright
and a Janette Stcere, every day.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The S.M. Herald and the irrepressible
<> Wingen” Abbott are now writing at great length to
point out that N.S. Wales should not float its loans
locally, but in London. “To borrow locally, simply to
keep the interest in the colony,” says the Herald, “is no
more sensible than to do work locally for the sake of
employing people if the cost to the State is thereby
increased.” As the State is the people this practically
means—supposing it does cost more to borrow in Aus-
tralia, which, by the way, is a mere assumption resting
on no visible evidence—that in the Herald’s opinion it is
better to pay 31 per cent, to somebody else than to pay
4 per cent, to oneself. As a crushing argument against
local borrowing the Herald points out that Treasurer
See’s 4 per cent, loan, issued at 100, is now worth
—which, by the way, it is not-so that the province has
lost £6 10s. on every £lOO. An equally painful and
equally relevant fact is that a 3£ per cent, loan floated,
not very long ago, in London at £9O 145., is now selling at
over £lOO, so that, in this instance, the province has lost
£9 6s. per £lOO.
As a matter of fact, the miserable province
always does lose on its borrowing transactions, and there
is no evidence to prove that it will lose more by borrowing
in Sydney than in London. Anyhow, a loan of £1,000,000
at 4 per cent, means the payment, in half a century, of
£2,000,000 in interest, and if the Herald considers that
Australia will be no better for keeping that amount in-
stead of sending it to a foreign country it only shows
what a vast and comprehensive fool that paper can make
of itself when it tries. The Bulletin believes that money
can be obtained as cheaply in Australia as in England,
but even if it can’t that fact makes no difference. It is
better to pay 4 per cent, to oneself than per cent, to
somebody at the other side of the world, especially if the
dividends are made subject to absentee-tax, so as to
prevent the debentures drifting into the hands of foreign
investors.
Wingen Abbott, by the way, bases his argu-
ment on the assertion that “to borrow in London is to
bring in fresh capital and increase local competition,
thus helping to bring down interest.” The statement
would be much more accurate if it were turned upside-
down. To borrow in London is to send out fresh capital,
and thus ultimately reduce local competition, and send
up interest. If the country borrows £10,000,000 to-day,
in the course of half a century, making no allowance for
compound interest, it sends away £20,000,000 in interest
alone, and this looks a lot more like taking capital out
than bringing it in. Australia has already sent back to
England nearly all its borrowed capital in interest, and
has to keep on sending it qver and over again for ever and
ever. The man who first invented the theory that bor-
rowing brings capital into the country was a short-sighted
mortal. Australia would have been one of the richest
lands on earth if it had never brought in any borrowed
capital. As it is, it bids fair to be among the poorest.
The ways of the reconstructed bank are past
finding out. When last heard of, the National Bank had
£1,570,000 in cash on hand, and £1,500,000 in liquid secu-
rities. This amount was vastly in excess of any possible
demands, and halt of it was bearing no interest whatever.
The National might have used at least £500,000 of it in
paying off reconstructed deposits and never missed it,
and by so doing it would have saved £22,500 a year in
interest and practically doubled its profits. The Victoria,
with £1,200,000 in cash, could have done likewise, and
also saved £22,500 per annum off its interest burden. Yet
neither institution makes a move
A foolish yearning after gigantic cash reserves,
which are of no present use, and yield no interest, and
only serve the same, purpose as a nightmare, is weighing
down the Australian banks. The craving has assumed
such an aggravated form that even to save the share-
holders £22,500 per annum the scared board of directors
won’t part with a fraction of the useless hoard.

Melbourne Trams have been rising steadily for
several weeks, and now a gleam of real joy comes along in
the shape of a 5 per cent. div. The div. is owing partly
to increased traffic returns and chiefly to a soulless grind-
ing-down of the miserable employes to the last possible
copper, but none the less it is the most promising piece
of news which Melbourne has heard for a long time past.
♦ * * *
A vigorous, but unsuccessful effort was made in
Vic. Assembly to exempt banks from the land-tax, and
subject them to income-tax only. Most of the Yic. banks
have stacks of land, but no net income worth mentioning,
so the purpose of the move was sufficiently obvious.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
Another colliers’ strike threatens at Newcastle,
N.S.W. The masters insist on a reduction of wages,
which are already down to the subsistence minimum,
considering that most of the men only work half-time ;
and starvation seems imminent either way. Newcastle
trade has fallen off so much during the past five years—
partly through successful competition in foreign markets,
and partly through the development of Victorian collieries
—that there is now a large surplus of the labor which easily
found employment in more prosperous days. There is
little doubt, how'ever, that most of the mines could well
continue to pay the present rates if the owners were
satisfied with a shade less profit.
♦ » * •
Yic. Assembly has decided, by 51 votes against
39, to exempt mutual life assurance societies from the
Land-tax, thus cutting another £lO,OOO per annum off the
anticipated revenue. The only redeeming features in the
business are that mutual-life (Mfices are certainly better
deserving of such relief than land-jobbing institutions,
which exist for purely speculative purposes, and that at
least one Vic. office will feel much better for the exemp-
tion.
• • * •
That baby institution, the National Bank of
Tasmania, reports having made a net profit in the past
six months of £4694. It spends £4561 on a little 6 per
cent, per an. div., and carries forward £133 to strengthen
its position. The National, with its capital of £152,000,
and its £22,500 of reserve, wouldn’t be much of a bank
anywhere except in peaceful Tasmania, but in that isle of
lotus-eaters where booms are few and mild, and panics are
of rare occurrence, and no one gives way to unseemly
haste, it does well enough.
• * * *
An hotel descended from the father to the
mother, to the son, and finally to the sister, death being
the cause; the freehold belonged to the brewery, and
the tenants having been homely people, had no lease.
Being desirous of selling, the brewers were applied
to for a lease, and, after much demur, one with
stringent clauses was granted. On objection being raised
to the clauses, it was promptly squelched, with the
remark, “That or nothing.” On compulsory acceptance,
a buyer was sought, and after many had been submitted,
one was approved. But before the brewers would sign
the transfer they insisted on receiving a bonus of £so—
at the time of the application, they were
advised of the intention to sell. Yet a “Brewery
Manager” concludes a letter to the Argus thus: “The
retail trade, if they speak honestly, will say that breweries
are cordial, just, and liberal, so long as the retailer keeps
to the straight track.”
• • * •
It would be interesting to learn what is the
exact present position of the Hillgrove and Armidale
Water-power Electric Co. This 00. had a private bill
passed by last N.S. Wales Parliament giving it water-
rights and the necessary authority, &c. In the bill it
was provided that electric power for working the Hill-
grove mines and light for lighting Hillgrove township
would be generated, and the co. was given 18 months in
which to carry the Act into effect. It is understood that
on the very last day allowed under the Act, it generated
enough electricity to feed some lamps, but the main object
of the Act—that of generating power—does not appear to
have been effected, nor up to the present does electric
power seem to have been supplied to any of the mines.
Do the promoters take for granted that because they
generated a certain amount of electricity they saved their
Act, and that they can produce the electric motive power
at their own sweet will? Did the Govt, send a repre-
sentative officer to inspect the works and ascertain if they
were completely erected and in working order by the
limited time allowed by the private Act, and if not, why
not? Syndicates have still an easy time in dealing with
Parliament.

When Sydney (Municipal) Electrio Lighting
Bill comes for second reading, the democratic party
should make every possible move to have the sixth clause
withdrawn. By it power is given to the Municipal Coun-
cil to hire or purchase tin- ligh' (in bulk) from any 00. or
syndicate, instead of erecting its own works and maun
factoring for itself. Should this clause be retained, it
will play right into the pockets of the Colo and Grose and
cuier grabsters, who would thus hold in their clutch the
wmle of the City of Sydney. It is more than suspected
that the balance of aldermanic influence is favorable to so-
called “private enterprise,” and the opposition to the
clause should therefore be all the more compact and
determined.

• * • •
This is how a certain well-known financier does
his business. The applicant for a loan is asked to furnish
full particulars, to leave documents, and to call again.
Should all be right, on the next visit he is informed that
a loan will be granted, but no definite sum is fixed, and
he is instructed to call a third time. At the final visit
the amount is settled, and the financier calls in his book-
keeper, who produces a mortgage or assignment of inter-
est in which the amount is not filled in. Then a cheque
is made out, payable to client’s order, but with the
amount blank. The financier signs this, and requests the
client to endorse it, “ so that the clerk can fill in the
amount and get the cash from the bank.” The bor-
rower endorses it accordingly, and the clerk leaves with
it. During his absence the deed is signed, still with the
amount in blank, “as the bookkeeper does all that
work the clerk returns, the money' is paid over, and
the borrower leaves. The cheque has never gone to the
bank, and it and the deed are now filled in for as large a
sum as the shecurity will stand. The cheque is then
taken to the bank and cashed in gold. By which com-
plication of devices the usurer has the evidence of the
signed deed and the endorsed cheque, and of himself and
his factotum and the bank ledger, against the bare testi-
mony of the borrower. Consequently, the borrower’s
attempt to dispute the claim is apt to end in his committal
for perjury.
• • * •
The Manufacturing Jewellers’ Association of
Melb. stamps all goods turned out by the members
according to the purity of the metal. The penalty for
any breach of the rule is £ 100; hut as the public have
never been made acquainted with the meaning of the
various stamps, it is a dead letter to them. So for want of
judicious advertising the desired effect is missed.
♦ * » *
A meeting of the creditors in the estate of a
Victorian country storekeeper was held recently, in Melb.
The estate showed about 3s. in the £. When the meeting
closed, one of the largest creditors spoke out: “Mr.
, I admire you, I regard you with profound respect.
You may be a poor man with a load of misfortune on
your shoulders, but the way you have gone through us is
sublime, and you are the biggest and most eternal rogue
among the adjective scoundrels of this blazing colony.”
This is the result of paying 3s. in the £. Tne man who
compounds for a halfpenny is never talked to like that.
• * * *
The Mutual Life Assurance Society of New
York has just got rid of its oldest annuitant. He bought
his annuity in ’65. Being then in his 74th year he should,
according to all the mortality tables, have lived seven
years and four months, but the veteran hung out and
drew his income for 28 years. When he died at the age of
102 he was over 3000 dollars to the good on the trans-
action.

* •
The last notorious miser who pegged out for a
better land left 150 bell-toppers behind him all full of
coppers. The coppers which had overflowed the bell-
toppers were stacked away in kegs. The only way to
make your money secure, nowadays, is to put it into
coppers ; then no burglar smaller than a camel can get
away with it.
• ♦ * •
A poor Melb. widow, in need of .£5 for an
operation on the eye of one of her five children, was
attracted by the advt. of “ a lady ” willing to lend on fur-
niture. The widow answered the advt. and got a reply
from a firm, who congratulated her on not falling into the
hands of the Jews. She paid an inspection-fee of ss. (and
6d. tram-fare) and got a “loan” of £7 25., of which £5
only was cash and £2 2s. a receipt (unstamped, and
thereby may hang a prosecution) for costs of bill-of-sale.
At the end of 19 months the widow had paid £lO to the
usurers, of which no part went to repay the principal, the
lenders claiming a balance due of £l5 for principal and
arrears of interest. The Herald put a solicitor on the
track, and £3 10s. was accepted in full of all demands.
When are Australian laws to be brought into line with
reason on the usury question?
* • «
Broken Hill mining information, as placed
before the readers of daily papers ought to be accu-
rate. It is the custom now to submit mining reports
in MS. to the managers to whose mines they refer: what
is afterwards left appears in print. The metropolitan
press mostly gets (1) a rehash, (2) a direotly-censorised
wire, or (3) a wire drafted by manager (or directors), and
handed to the meek scribe in place of his own wire, which,
submitted for censorship, has not found favor with the
salt of the earth, and has been cast aside.

J. B. Tanquet, of N.S.W. Mines Depart-
ment, writes a sensible report on Mt. Drysdale gold-field,
showing that the gold occurs in the country-rock, and
is not defined in line or walls. It is most desirable that
surface-prospecting should go on. Mt. Drysdale is the
latest N.S.W. gold-puzzle.

Announced that the general manager of the
Wentworth mine got a cable saying that the half-yearly
meeting held in London “passed off satisfactorily, there

and divs. Why should there be any opposition ? Gold-

♦ ♦ * *
The New Bushman’s Hill (N.S.W.) is a good
little mine worked on a tribute which expires at the end of
the coming month. But the claim is not all that news-
papers make it appear when they talk of “plenty of
stone,” &c. The vein runs from 6 to 16in. in hard diorite,
which, however, as a rule shoots away to a slide, enabling
the ground to be broken inexpensively. Where the vein
bulges it is usually poorer, but the average within the
gold-shoot is about 2oz.
* * * *
That sterling and consistent property, Mitchell’s
Creek Freehold, N.S.W., is henceforth to be worked for
something like what it is supposed to be worth, the
owners having spent, in addition to the purchase-money,
something like £30,000 in plant and development. The
lode has been opened out and partially worked for fully
4000 ft., and should return substantial profits for long to
come.
* * * *
The Lackey Mine, Sunny Corner, has put out
another SOOoz. crushing and declared a big syndicale-div.
A few more good consecutive returns, and one more
N.S.W. gold-mine worthy the name will be added to the
brief list of regular gold-producers.
