Vic. gold-yield for 1894, 670,5440 z., making the
total product to date, 59,443,1020 z.
• • • •
The West Volunteer P.A., started at Lefroy in
April, ’92, in 32,500 shares of 3s. each, had, until 16th
Oct. last, most discouraging results. Since that date it
has cut the lode at the greatest depth for the field, and
taken out 172 tons which gave a cake of 19500 z. gold,
valued at £7BOO. It is practically “a poor man’s show,”
and its directors capable “men of the people,” and to
them, and the pluck and persistency of Mr. “ Jack ”
Lyall, the legal manager, this result is due: The mine
has a grand body of stone averaging 3ft. wide, which
underlies about Ift. in 12.
♦ * • •
The reported discovery of true diamonds in the
New Donaldson mine, Corinna (Tas.), and an alleged
similar find in sand in the Arthur River, some time ago,
look very like the fulfilment of a prognostication made,
years since, by the late Govt. Geologist, F. Thureau,
F.G.S., that diamonds would be found in the coastlands
near Mt. Sedgwick. That the whole of the country away
back from the West Coast of Tasmania is rich
in minerals is now well-proved—silver, lead, tin, copper,
and gold being liberally distributed. And, now, dia-
monds. In spite of Mt. “ Hoaxley,” more money has
been made by small and steady investors out of solid
gold-mining in quiet little Tasmania of late years than on
some of the much-advertised big gold fields of Australia.
An interesting article could be written on the ups-and-
downs, and marginal chances of small piles within the
reach of modest purses, that have taken place within the
last few years in the share-market of small Tasmanian
ventures—low-priced quotations at which a big gamble*
would disdain to look, but which have spelled “ com-
petencies ” lor the steady and watchful speculator.
• * • •
Mount Lyell (Tas.) silver-mine has £60,000 in
hand, which is being devoted to erecting a reduction
plant and building a tramway. For the past six months
325 tons of ore raised have yielded 329,0320 z. silver.
* • * *
Mt. Morgan’s half-yearly 0utput—49,3650z.,
value £205,523; divs. £150,000. This mine is about to
make some important trials in treating low-grade ores at
a total cost of about 12s. a ton.
• * •
Tasmania has, since ’73, produced 71,094 tons of
tin, valued at £6,122,434. It is the third largest producer
of tin in the world. The Straits, first: Cornwall, second.
• • • •
Brilliant Block, Charters Towers, 1184 tons for
8650 z. Since opened, 36,626 tons for 39,7620 z. ; £36,250
paid in divs.—the bulk during last year.
* * * *
The attempt of Col. North to induce the London
public to find £700,000 for the Londonderry has been a
frost, as not nearly the amount of cash was subscribed,
though they profess to have issued “letters of regret,”
No doubt the clever Nitrate King is on the right side of
the hedge, for he had the capital underwritten ; but the
unfortunates who underwrote large amounts will not be
best pleased to find that they have to provide all the
necessary coin to pay the vendors. A more audacious
attempt to make an unheard-of profit has never been
known, remarks The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
Fancy asking a price that would leave North and Fingall
a clear profit of £344,000! North’s rapacity is well-known
in connection with Nitrates, but this bangs Banagher.
♦ * * »
One reason why every Australian gold-mine
floated in England has a vast, unwieldy capital, so large
that it makes divs. all but hopeless, is that the English
investor has a secret love for such things. He regards t he
stupendous capital as being, somehow or other, a proof of
the solidity of the enterprise, and no amount of experi-
ence seems to convince him to the contrary. His utterly
wooden-headed fatuousness in this respect should do a
lot to destroy the Australian’s reverence for him as a
financial authority, but somehow it doesn’t, and the
opinions of this person who doesn’t know how to come in
out of the wet are still bumptiously cabled to Australia
as matters of serious importance. Altogether, it is diffi-
cult to decide whether the muddled creature at the other
end of the wire, who sends out his financial criticisms on
things which he doesn’t understand, or the muddled
creature at this end, who receives them with such solemn
reverence, is the more pitiable.
♦ • * »
There are now 67 Westralian gold-mining com-
panies actually registered in London, and probably as
many which haven’t reached the registration stage. These
67 companies have an aggregate capital of £4,172,000 ;
they employ 57 highly-paid secretaries, 250 highly-paid
directors, and 500 or 600 clerks and other employes with
boiled shirts and high, stiff collars, and they occupy 34
exceedingly swell offices at high rentals. The bursting of
the Westralian gold-boom should be near at hand. No
country can long stand this sort of thing.
• ♦ » *
Ttie original owners of the Londonderry are to
receive their £170,000 cash in Melbourne towards the end
of the month, whereupon the mine will be taken over, and
the British shareholders will soon learn what they have
got for their money. The condition of the sale is that the
rich faces disclosed, and now sealed up, are not to
be disturbed until the money and shares are paid over.
♦ » ♦ *
There is said to be some little difficulty
in London as to the area of the Londonderry
mine. It is alleged that it was bought and sold as con-
taining 25 acres, the area applied for; but when surveyed
it was found that the prospectors had pegged too little,
and the whole of the surrounding ground being taken up,
the area had to be brought down to about 16a. Now, this
is the very sort of thing that people in London, who are
not in the promoters’ deal, would like to lay hold of as a
legitimate excuse for law or blackmail.
* * » •
Bayley’s shares are now selling in London at
about 14s. 6d., and as it is very difficult for the brokers
to carry over the stock beyond fortnightly or monthly
settlements, faith in the mine has departed. But for the
London register the shares would be down to 55., and
from what one hears of the general appearance of the
mine that is about their worth. Already, even with
increased water and stamps, the management find it a
tough job to put out 700 oz. a week.
* * • •
In the last report of Bayley’s No. 1 South there
appears a telegram, dated 26th Dec. : “ Struck very rich
coarse gold in stopes over the 100 ft. level.” Nothing
more. Somebody afar off tells Tub Bulletin that this
strike was so rich that about IOOOoz. of gold were picked
out in two hours. Where were the Coolgardie correspon-
dents, and why hasn’t the news been published ere now?
No. 1 South has its battery and water ready. The result
of the first crushing must be read in the light of this
alleged pocket af wonderful ore, which has come in the
nick of time. There is a big heap of quartz at grass, and
it has been said that this heap will, in the bulk, go “ some
ounces to the ton.” Several people who have seen it
think they know better.
♦ * * *
Fortune of War : “ Mine looking poor—loo
tons crushed for 300 z.” When the battery starts the
truth must be told. The machine cannot lie—much. So
it will be with nine-tenths of the mines at Coolgardie ;
when their batteries are erected and the dams are full and
all chance for excuse is gone, the magnitude of the fairy-
tales will be realised.

Again The Bulletin asks, whab has become of
the Wealth of Nations mine 7 Are they picking out
thousands of ounces to make a big average bulk crushing ?

Mt. Burgess people rushed up their battery at
Coolgardie in the quickest time on record. Their ore is
low grade, but they have a big body, and the mine should
pay.

“ Plenty of gold is coming out of the lower
levels of Bayley’s.” So runs a recent newspaper-wire.
Some day Bayley’s may strike really “ big ” gold below ;
and then the public who have grown weary of the oft-
repeated crams about the richness of the lower levels,
won’t behave it.
• • • •
Brookman and Lane, the rulers of the Cool-
gardie Syndicate mines at Hannan’s, are to put their
heads together tn re a comprehensive scheme to deliver
water from Hannan’s Lake to their claims, about three
miles away.
• • • •
The Dollars, L.S.D., and British Empire line
of reef, writes an expert correspondent, is 8 miles north
of Yalgo, W.A. The reef runs from 2to 3ft. wideband is
developed by various shafts to a depth of about 100 ft.
The Dollar and L.S.D. are owned in Melbourne and will
mill abo'’‘ an oz. to the ton. They have a good length
of ore-sin. .c. The British Empire quartz is not so good,
a late milling test giving only lOdwta.
The Melville district is 14 miles N. of Yalgo.
The chief mines work a reef running up to eight-feet
thick. The grade is low, from sto lOdwts.
The Island, at Lake Austin, has two parallel
lines of reef consisting of the hungry-looking white sto ie
so common in Westralia, with bunches of rich gold quartz
in pockets through the veins. The mines are the Golconda
and the Chicago. Outside the few rich pockets found in
these reefs the quartz is poor, so that no difficulty in
picking the ore to any richness presents itself.
• * * »
The Mainland Consolidated, three miles N. of
the Island, has a similar formation. The claims are
known as the Mainland Dalys, the Last Chance, and the
Blocks, and have produced among them about 5000 oz
daring the last 18 months. The ore-shoots are very short,
some 6 to 10ft., and the richest ore occurs in pockets in
these. Development has engendered the suspicion that
gold will diminish with depth.

Melbourne is being actively exploited by all the
Westralian wild-eat-mongers who can’t raise the passage-

•*• • ~
The secretary of Coolgardie hospital writes the
Eastern press with a view of di -proving the alarmist state-
ments made as to the hospital being crowded with fever
patients. He states that at the date of his letter (Dec.
14th) there were but 20 cases of fever, none dangerous,
and that no deaths had occurred during the previous
week from that disease. He does not, however, set o< t
the accommodation of the hospital, in which, at latest,
there were 22 patients.
* * * *
The late strike at Hannan’s appears to have been
a godsend to the Boulder crowd, whose 24 leases are said
not to have yet given enough gold to pay for the men’s
water. Over 200 men were employed, and the leaders in
the strike had determined beforehand to throw up their
job and go prospecting. But it was rough on the po >r
devils who had just been taken on.
♦ * * •
The Perseverance (Hannan’s) has just been sold
to an English syndicate. It was originally taken up by
Pung, Rose and party, who also had the" Black Crow,
now known as Star of the East. They abandoned the
latter, thinking it no good ; then the present proprietors
got hold of it. While MTntosh was floating the Per-
severance, in Melbourne, the holders dollied for wages out
of the reef.
Parkes didn’t attend the Sydney Com-
mercial Travellers’ banquet. He absolutely
loathes drummers’ after-dinner stories.
Dec. Review of Reviews awkward
name—heads an interview with Mr. Service with
the statement that W.A. has a nominee second
chamber, which will considerably astonish the
score of councillors elected a few months ago
under “the changed constitution. Even when the
Review brings its history up to date, and alleges,
in regard to the recent Q. Assembly division which
kept the Govt, in office by a majority of one,
that “ the Labor members shrunk from appearing
before their constituents . . . two of them
remained outside the bar of the House,” it is not
more happy. A more malicious fib it would be
hard to utter, seeing that every Labor member
polled his vote, and that the two who stayed out-
side the bar were Govt, supporters. But the
Australian section of the Review now stinks of
high-Toryism afar off.
Cabled that “Journeyman Judge” Wren-
fordsley (who humped bluey all over Australia in
the hope of finding a permanent billet and an easy
pension), suggests that a Federal Council for Aus-
tralia should be constituted from the existing
Legislative Councils ! The ghastly ignorance of
Australasian sentiment expressed in such a propo-
sition should entitle Mr. Wrenfordsley to a leather
medal in any competition open only to superlative
idiots. Almost as well might the ex-Judge sug-
gest to the voters of Australia that a Federal
Council should be constituted of such British peers
as happen to have visited the country.
In last week’s Bulletin was reprinted a
statement, which had apparently been widely cir-
culated in the provincial press, to the effect that a
popular Sydney suburban station-master was, when
in Britain, the superior officer of Chief Commis-
sioner Eddy. The Bulletin is assured that there
is no truth in the statement, and, as it appears to
have caused annoyance to the station-master in
question, who is in no way responsible for its cir-
culation, we have to express our regret for its pub-
lication.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback - the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex
trashed, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory mav be had by nnv'uio who maker
a continuous use of Uncle Toby's Rolled Oats.

Personal Items.
“Sammy” Lees is the first printer who has
sat in the Sydney mayoral chair.
There is some talk of F. W. Ward, now
of Brisbane Courier, returning to Sydney.
Jack Edwards, of yore, was the first Aus-
tralian native in the Victorian Parliament.
Mr. Francis Kenna, of Brisbane, a name
familiar to Bulletin readers, will shortly publish
a volume of verse.
Charles Smith, son of journalist James
Smith, and quondam sub-editor of the Argus, is
now editor of Ballarat Star.
The biggest book in the genealogical bay
Melb. Public Library is “The History of the
O’Toole Family ” (!), which is exactly 4in. thick.
The acting N.S.W. Supreme Court judge-
ship, in the absence of Justice Stephen, will, it is
whispered, be conferred upon Mr. Frank Rogers,
Q.C.
Ex-Mayoress Yates, of Onehunga (M.L.;,
is expected over in Australia soon to lecture on
the New Woman. But Mrs. Y. is a back
number.

HEXRY LAWSON.
N.S.W. ex-Premier Dibbs occasionally
writes leading articles for a country paper, but
they can’t be recognised owing to the deletion of
the adjectives.
Gladstone’s assured income is £25,000 a
year, and yet he writes “poems.” This latter
trait, however, arises from a childish ambition to
emulate Parkes.
The editor of an Australian comic journal
some time deceased, now runs a metropolitan firm,
the chief business of which is to provide compe-
tent accoucheuses.
Robert Louis Stevenson is credited with
having smoked 400 cigarettes daily. He died of
brain-paralysis, and no wonder —if he smoked
only 100 cigarettes daily.
The wife of a Sydney suburban bailiff has
gone to England to prosecute her alleged very
strong claim to a seven-million estate which has
been in Chancery 63 years.
Dr. Philson, an old army surgeon at Auck-
land (M.L.), is the only coroner in Australasia
who does not willingly accept a “temporarily
insane ” verdict in suicide cases.
Ernest Hosking, the spook - exposing
juggler, is a compositor by trade. As times go,
had he followed his trade, he might now have
been a spook instead of a spook-raiser.
Pastor Jefferis, since his return to Ade-
laide, has failed to make any stir in the religious
world. Dishwater could not be duller than his
present career, despite much advertising.
The library of Australian books recently
bought by Chief Justice Way from Mr. E. A.
Petherick is that originally owned by the pious
South Melbourne swindler, Matthias Larkin.
Lewis Cohen, moneylender and ex-Mayor
of Adelaide, much scandalised his brother Jews
recently by suggesting the formation of a choir of
male and female voices in the local Synagogue.
When asked in N.S.W. Assembly the
other night whether it was true that he once
threatened to put Major-General Hutton under
arrest, ex-Premier Dibbs replied, “Oh, very
likely.”
Barrister-preacher Field, of Sydney, says
that ladies who enter with zest into society enter-
tainments cannot be Christians. But if the pro-
fessional lawyer can be a Christian, surely any-
bpdy can be one !
Mr. Tom Cue, after whom the capital of
the Murchison is named, is a burly six-foot pros-
pector of 20 years’ experience. His octogenarian
father still lives, hale and hearty, at Casterton,
Western Victoria.
Penrose Julyan, who is about to resign his
position as London financial adviser to the Maori-
land Govt., is known to fame chiefly as the in-
ventor of the marine steam flour-mill and floating
bakery used in the Crimean war.
It is averred that Yiscount Drumlanrig,
eldest son of the Marquis of Queensberry, whose
accidental death was lately announced, committed
suicide. He recently became engaged, whereupon
two other women threatened him.
The Mayoress of a Vic. country town has
a brogue you could lay bricks with. Gov. Hope-
toun and wife visited her dominions, and a ball
was given in their honor. Says the Countess :
“Do you find it warm, Mrs. M’Gillicuddy?”
“Ah, yis,” replied the lady Mayoress, “and
you’re doin’ a schweat yerself, Oi notice.”
It will interest the poor in spirit to know
that Bishop Kennion, late of Adelaide, now of
Bath and Wells, has bought a lot of furniture
from Lord Hervey, and that his palace is expected
to rival the most luxurious in England.
Mr. Justice Simpson, the newly-appointed
of N.S.W. Supreme Court, started life as a clerk
in N.S.W. Lands Office. Through the elevation
of Mr. Simpson, Julian Salomons, Q. 0., admitted
in ’59, becomes the father of the N.S.W. Bar.
Sydney Telegraph sums up Robert Louis
Stevenson (whom it killed at 52, instead of 44):
“He was preparing a salad when he died. He
was buried on the summit of Vaca Mountain,
1300 ft. above the level of the sea.” This is fame !
English critics gush over an Australian
story called “The Pilgrims,” written by Carlton
Dawes, “a big, square-shouldered Australian, with
dark hair, and a massive, kindly face, shielded
with glasses.” “Carlton Dawes” —who is the
gentleman ? The Bulletin fears he is English.
Mr. J. A. Hill, whose portrait was printed
in The Bulletin 18 months ago, now claims to
be the sole survivor of the little band who watched
Gov. Hindmarsh proclaim S.A. a British province
on 28 Deo., 1836. He was present at the latest
celebration at the old spot. Though 75 years old,
he is still hearty.
“At the Colonial Institute, Mr. Gillies
looked well and full of youthful promise. Gazing
at him one cannot help wishing that some mil-
lionaire would adopt him and send him to a public
school and to the University, for he could not fail
to grow up to be a remarkable man.”— Argus
correspondent.
A Yic. Supreme Court Judge was present
in the front row at one of Mrs. Besant’s Melb.
lectures in which she enlarged on the wrongness of
judicial murder. She said afterwards that per-
ceiving that a legal-looking gentleman in front
looked very troubled when she broached the sub-
ject she enlarged on it purposely.
Gov. Kintore is an elocutionist, and read
his graceful farewell to the Parliament and people
of S. A. so pathetically at the close of his proroga-
tion speech that the more impressionable poli-
ticians wept, while several hard faces pretended
to do so. The departing Earl is the most popular
Viceroy that Adelaide has yet done obeisance to.
A certain great N.S. Wales snake-
authority, sate him down at Blayney the
other day, and wrote to S.M. Herald that he
was about to poison himself. Then he drank a
bottle of whisky—and got two months’ gaol. The
poor man must have been mad. To suppose that
nny snake-expert raised in the Burragorang Valley
could die of one bottle of whisky!
Ex-rev. and ex-M.L. A. J. F. Cullen filled
for a time the highest office in N.S.W. Congrega-
tionalism and flourished as designate Cabinet col-
league of Parkes. Now he is a suburban journalist.
Again, a Sydney suburban man was once a manu-
facturer on a large scale of perfumes and flavoring-
essences. Now he is an inspector of nuisances.
Queer world.
Innes Lynch, who died recently at Temora
(N.S.W.), aged 62 years, had been a British army
officer—captain of heavy dragoons at the age of
19 ! served in the Crimean war ; was taken from a
Young (N.S.W.) gold-eommissionership to fight
against the Maoris ; was military commandant in
Fiji in the time of King Thakombau ; editor of a
N.S.W. country paper ; and—district court bailiff,
in which occupation Death levied on the process-
server.
ON A XMAS HOLIDAY .
The clever H. H. Champion is now per-
manently located at Melbourne. His imaginary
“ gentlemanliness ”is his weak point. He wants
to run with the democratic hare and hunt with
the upper-middle-class hounds. The result is that
he is too much of a right-thinking person to be a
reliable socialist, and too much of a socialist to be
an acceptable “gentleman.”
Parson Bevan, the Melb. long woolled
merino, developes a most surprising likeness to
Henrik Ibsen. Some people lay themselves out
for these resemblances. Hewlett Ross, secretary
of the newly-formed Melb. Fabian Society, bears
a most remarkable resemblance to some of the
portraits of Shakespeare. Up to now, however,
the resemblance has not broken out inside, as it
were.
Young Longstaff, the Yic. “travelling-
scholarship” artist, has sent along to Melb.
National Gallery, on loan, a beautiful portrait of
a lady that would do credit to any painter on
earth. The sitter—who stands in the picture—
is doubtless a brainy, soulful nervous subject of
the Ellen Terry type. Longstaff seems to have
accentuated each charm of her poetic face, and the
lady who smiles at one from the canvas is almost
too interestingly natural for this life.
Apropos of the endeavor to upset the will
of the late Professor Holloway, pill magnate, on
behalf of a nephew. That nephew’s brother was
recently a Victorian country storekeeper, and
some years ago, it is related, went ’Ome to visit
Uncle Holloway with a view to getting monetary
assistance for the extension of his business. The
“ Professor ” entertained him royally, listened
with interest to all his plans, and on his departure
lectured him on the virtues of industry and per-
severance, and presented him with —a box of pills !

Captain M‘lntosh, of the ill-fated Waira-
rapa, owned a Scotch terrier which invariably
accompanied him on his voyages. Prior to the
last departure of the boat, the captain and his dog
were staying with a St. Kilda friend, but when
the time came for sailing the dog was missing, and
the ship that never returned pursued her course to
Barrier Island without it. When she had been
gone a day or two, however, Toby re-appeared
at St. Kilda, and has been there ever since.
It is printed that long ago T. S. Mort,
attracted by a letter of G. H. Reid’s in the news-
paper, “sought him out and became one of his
intimate friends,” and on one occasion when
entertaining a large parly of guests at “ Green-
o vkes,” he directed a lady’s attention to a young
man at the other end of the drawing-room.
“That young man,” said Mort, “will one day be
Premier of N.S.W.” The point of the story is
that it was told by Georgey-Porgey himself to a
(Cosmos) interviewer.
Premier Reid, of N.S.W., is credited with
being able to sleep at will. Mr. Reid, who has
lately spoken of himself as “a youngster,” denies
that he is 52—he will be 50 this year. Apropos of
which Sydney Star remarks that Napoleon died
at 52, and Alexander the Great at 33, Hannibal’s
age when he crossed the Alps. At 45, Caesar con-
quered Britain, and Pitt died at 47. There have
been half-a-dozen younger Premiers in Australia.
Mr. Kingston is at present but 44, Forrest, 47.
Griffith (Premier at 38) is now 49 and Chief Jus-
tice of Queensland.
Mrs. Helen Stannard, who fills library
shelves with third-rate novels under the pen-name
of “John Strange Winter,” thinks she disposes of
the proofs of the intellectual inferiority of woman
by an argumentum ad hominem directed against
Mr. Hall Caine. She says she has married and
reared children, sold 1J million books, and made
a general success of life ; while H.C. is a frail man
who, when-Ac writes a book, is thoroughly ex-
hausted. “Therefore,” says Mrs. S., “I fail to
see where his immense superiority over me comes
in.” Well, it comes in the utter inferiority of the
lady’s books. Fifty thousand “John Strange
Winters” could not write one “Bondman.”
Says a Bulletin corres-
pondent ; In view of the as-
sertions made with regard to
the wealth taken by Mrs.
Besant from Australia, a plain
statement of facta will interest
Bulletin readers. Almost
every penny of Mrs. Besant’s
earnings as a lecturer, under
the Smythe regime, goes to
cancel the liabilities of the
Theosophical Literature Pub-
lishing Fund, in London.
Mrs. Besant is the great
money-earner of the Theoso-
phical Society, and as it was
mainly at her instance that
the society incurred the great
expense of publishing H.P.B. ’s
“Secret Doctrine” and other
works, she felt that it was her
primary duty to liquidate, by
her own efforts, the debts thus
incurred. With regard to her
share of the “ enormous ” pro-
ceeds of her recent lectures in
Australia, it is sufficient to
say that she had not the least
idea that such a monetary
value attached to her dis-
courses as has proved to be
the case. For instance, her
proportion of the Sydney pro-
ceeds was <£79 17s. 7d., of
which all save £5 was re-
mitted to Mrs. Coeper-Oakley,
at the Theosophical Society’s
literary dep.it in Loudon, and
the £5 went in douceurs to
servants, steamboat-stewards,
and such likeinevitable para-
sites of the traveller. —G.
Henry Burton Bradley, the oldest practis-
ing solicitor in Australia, died at Sydney last
week in his 80th year. He came to Australia with
his uncle, the late Judge Burton, of the N.S.W.
Supreme Court, to whom he was clerk for many
years. He acted in that capacity, it is understood,
at the memorable trial of John Knatchbull for the
murder of Mrs. Jamieson in Kent st., Sydney.
There is yet an older attorney, though retired from
practice—Mr. B. O. Rodd, of Five Dock, who was
admitted as far back as 1833. It is singular that
Mr. Bradley, who devoted all his spare time to
sanitary reform, should die of typhoid.
While the greasy panegyrist was at work
on the N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges in the cur-
rent Review of Reviews, he might surely have
given a statement of the pleasing and dignified
methods adopted in the Mount Rennie trial by
His Honor Mr. Justice Windeyer. A bare re-
cital of the circumstances attendant on that,
memorable affair would have been enough to for
ever embastion the judicial reputation of the
learned and lucky personage who (amongst Ids
other shining qualities) is, we read, “just a trifle
too suspicious to be imposed on.” Spooks of Cisam
and Josephine 1
Photo, by Eden] [Sydney.
Mr. HARRY GOODWIN.
Harry Goodwin (whose portrait appears in
this issue) is champion of Sydney Amateur Walk-
ing Club, and, if all goes well, means to be
amateur champion walker of Australasia. Since
21st Sept., 1893, when his walking career com-
menced, Goodwin has started in seven handicaps,
finishing five times Ist, once 2nd, and once 3rd.
On the grass track at Kensington Race-
course this year he covered If mile in 13rnin,
41|secs, At Sydney Harrier Sports on Aug. 25 he
did the mile in 7min. 2secs, ; and on Sept. 22, at
the Public Schools Sports, the same distance in
6min. 34secs. ; both performances on grass. The
latter time (6*34) is 25 seconds inside the Aus-
tralian record, but Goodwin’s application for
a record was disallowed because but two time-
keepers took the race; the Amateur Athletic
Association requiring three. On Nov. 15 Good-
win walked 1,2, and 3 miles for a record, on N.
Sydney Reserve, and was successful at each dis-
tance. He did the mile in 7'6 4-5, 2 miles in 15*44-5,
and 3 miles in 23-13 l-sseos. The 3 miles record
was disallowed owing to two cautions having been
administered, and the two-mile record is at present
under review by the council of the Amateur
Athletic Association.
Roger Tichborne graduated at the Jesuits
college at Stonyhurst and served two years in the
Dragoon Guards. Yet in 1866 the Claimant wrote
to Lady Tichborne, his “dear mother,” a letter
containing this :
1 do not wicsh any person to know me in this Country
When I take my proper prosttrion and title. Having
therefore made up my mind to return and face the Sea
once more, I must request you to send me the Means of
doing so, and paying a fue outstanding debts, 1 would
return by the overland Mail. The passage money and
other expenees would be over two Hundred pound, for I
propose Sailing from Victoria, not this Colainly. And to
Sjil from Melbourne in my own name. Now to annable
me to do this, my dear Mother, you must send me
Again :
If to late i will send this to England has the English
mail does not close for two hours after the Mail for
France. I have enclosed a photograph of my self that
you may see how greatly I have improve. Hoping, my
dear mama, to see alive once more. But lam afraid not,
has I can not get suefioine Money to come home with.
Goody bye, my Dear Mama, and' MAY THE BLESSED
MARIA HAVE MERCY ON YOUR SOUL.-I Remain,
your Affectionate Son, Roger Charles Tichborne.
When the poor old lady at last met her bulky
bogus son she declared that “ his face was like his
father’s, but he had ears like his uncle’s.”
Sydney Truth publishes (for the first time) an
interesting statement to the effect that it was the
unanimous judgment of the Australian lawyers
(including the clever Isaacs) engaged in diagnosing
the case, that the Claimant was not Arthur
Orton, but a natural son of the late Sir Thomas
Tichborne, his mother being one of the members
of the Orton family. This conclusion was based
on, among other things, his undoubted resem-
blance to both families, and his acquaintance with
their personal secrets.

Legislative Oouneil with a long-felt Jack Want.

crawfish—backing out. The Crawfish Chamber 1

British New Guinea last year cost Queens-
land £15,000, and returned to its Treasury £5886.
Imports amounted in value to £28,501; exports
to £14,952.
The Shea’s Creek unemployed relief
scheme, which has cost N.S.W. 200,000 lovely
sovereigns, seems chiefly to consist in digging a
canal through a quicksand.
An ex-M.P. (Labor branch) was seen in
Sydney streets, the other day, with his coat off,
and was effusively greeted and shaken hands with
by all passing acquaintances.
When a Premier is the legal adviser of a
firm of forgers who get into trouble he is placed
in a delicate position and should see that his
law-officers move circumspectly.

am not in sympathy with the employment of
colored labor on mail steamers, but that is all I
can be expected to say.” Why is it all ?

The Mayor of Hamilton (Vic.) presided at
a meeting in favor of minimum wage. Then his
Council censured him. Whereupon the rate-
payers filled the town-hall and unanimously cen-
sured the Council.
The Argus, on Retrenchment, faintly re-
calls Stout, the Political Economist, in Bulwer’s
“Money.” “They give their Lord Chancellor
ten thousand a year, when I have a second-cousin
who will do it for a quarter of the sum.”
“ A Second Chamber acts as a drag. Pro-
gress is always uphill work. So we are at pains to
provide a drag beforehand —for an uphill journey.
There, in one word, you have the whole philo-
sophy of Second Chambers.’’—Grant Allen.
The pastoralists in Queensland are really
considering the question of a further re 1 notion in
shearers’ wages during next season. The Bush
Workers’Union is “on its uppers” financially,
and the squatters know their opportunity is a good
one.
Armidale (N.S.W.) Express calls upon
local “selectors and land-owners” to petition the
Legislative Council against the land-tax. No
need for such a waste of breath. As well petition
bookmakers to use their influence against the
tote.

Bill.

ticians desires to be in the next Ministry. Solo
mon, Moule, and Hague all want the Treasurer-

V ictorians, but does not penalise other absentee
landlords. It might drive away capital! Yet
even the akgus admits that an influx of British
capital into Australia has always been attended
by disaster.

ported by a barrister, to pass Yic.’s most Demo-
cratic Bill (Land-Tax) through the Assembly.
Turner and Isaacs have succeeded in piecing
together a Liberal Ministry (so far as policy goes)

Andrew Clarke offers to do Duncan Gillies’
work as Vic. Agent-General for rather less than
half the money. Consider the lengths to which
this may be carried. Lord Nozoo is ready to take
the Governorship for a tenth of Hopetoun’s wage.
Nay, Longtnore would replace Turner —and so on.

in fighting for the veteran scientists Von Mueller
and Ellery. They also held out successfully for
an increase in the miserable Gov. pittance to the
Yic. artists. As far as Vie. Parliament is con-
cerned, Labor is distinctly hand in hand with art
and science.
The rival policies of retrenchment in the
S. A. Parliament lately have amounted to this.
The Labor party have determined that no one
drawing less than £2OO shall suffer. The Conser-
vatives have been specially interested to save men
with more than £6OO annually. The middle rank
has had no saviour.

£1403 is the trifle of taxpayers’ coin
annually pouched by four members of one Sydney
family for services which wouldn’t in the aggre-
gate fetcli £5 a week in open commercial market.
One of the quartette takes nearly .£5OO a year for
doing literally nothing, and another <£B a week for
clerical work that would be well-paid at 30s.
Mrs. Ida Halloran, 8.A., seeks to invest
a constituency with the power of recalling, by a
majority vote equal to that which elected him, a
member of Parliament whom it has ceased to trust.
The only reason against-this is -thtl iateiHiiliable
agitation which such a power might engender.
Three years is certainly too long a tether to give
the average member.
Member Crick’s desire that, in the event of
the Privy Council being thrown open to “colonial”
judges, no Australian Judge who has not occupied
the bench for ten years shall be eligible for
appointment, directly disqualifies the three Chief
Justices, Darley, Madden and Griffith, and
narrows the choice down to almost a one-man
field.
Major-General Hutton (N.S.W.) put his
side of a contentious topic thus a few nights
ago:—“What do you want? You want your
men trained according to the best standards.
Well, am 1 to send the men to England to be
trained, or am I to bring from England trained
men to instruct those here? Which is the more
economical, the more patriotic? Which shows
the sounder Australian spirit?”
The Australasian declares hysterically
that nothing like Premier Turner's land-tax
scheme “ has been heard in English history since
Edward I. extorted treasure from the Jews by
pulling their teeth till they produced their last
penny.” The Tory rag has no sense of proportion,
and mistakes the very ordinary worm in its back-
yard for the sea-serpent, simply because it is its
own worm.
Dear Bulletin, —If Dan O’Connor, or any
other Irishman, had been N.S.W. Postmaster-
General and done the letter-trick (opening letters
suspected to contain gambling communications)
now going on in the province, hundreds of the
Cook ilk would see in it a Jesuitical plot to upset
the Constitution. I consider that when a man
stamps a letter and posts it, he has a right to
have it delivered.—English-Australian.
Abolish Hansard 1 Why, the daily press
not only prepares each its special party-mystery
stew of Parliamentary proceedings, but their
recording angels actually omit therefrom the
green fat and titbits that relieve the dreary
horrors of long sittings. To abolish Hansard
would be to destroy the only life-resembling photo-
graphs of the uncouth animals in the public
menagerie.
Premier Reid ukases that no strangers
are to be employed on the N.S.W. Public Works
contracts. By this rascally piece of Protectionism,
this restricting of the labor market, labor-rates
are to be advanced (or, what is the same thing,
prevented from falling), and the poor suffering
country deprived of the advantages obtainable
from free-competition and the unfettered operation
of the sacred laws of supply and demand.
The repeated public statements of N.S.W.
Ministers Gould and Reid, asserting in effect that
only for the obstinate hostility of Justice Win-
deyer directions would be given for the release of
Francis Abigail, are unfavorably commented on in
judicial circles. It is held to be impolitic for a
Government to express sympathy in one direction
and focus public attention on a particular person
as the cause of a contrary course of action.

The “cheap food ” D.T. among the very prophets

Now that N.S.W. Railway Commissioner
Eddy is about to retire there are hints that ex-

serenely from below. Mr. Goodchap was a good
man who never had Mr. Eddy’s chances. But he
is not so young as he was. He has, to his friends’
delight, recovered from a terrible illness, and he
has a nice pension of £B6O a year. If he is a wise
man, he «ill stick to it. Only men who want to
die soon take N.S.W, Chief-Commissionerships of
Railways.

Dear Bulletin, —To compel landowners
to make their own valuations for land-tax does
not wholly abolish land-resumption “makes.” A
man I knew owned an estate in Maoriland. He
put it in an agent’s hands for sale at £3500.
Hearing that the place would be required for a
battery-site, at the next property-tax valuation he
valued it at £19,000, which the Govt, assessor
accepted with delight. When the resumption
took place the Govt, was presented with the
valuation which their own officers had accepted.
The owner got £lB,OOO, or thereabouts.— Maori
Babe. [He would probably have got £60,000 if
there had been no land-tax valuation. —Ed. B.]

Gov. Kinfcore, who departs in a few weeks,
has privately stated that he leaves S. A. with
about .£5OOO profit on his four years' stay. It is
Certainly a small sum, considering that during
the last two years Govt. House has been virtually

Recognising the solemn responsibility of this added

The “open sesame” to the N.S. W. Civil
Service is, notwithstanding the alleged competitive-
examination test, the “crimson thread of kinship”
with an M.P., or an M. P.’s jackal. The pass-
word to promotion is in the nature of the answer
that can be made to either of the questions —“How
old is your mother?” “How old is your grand-
mother?’’ In N.S.W., the Masonic phrase, “ the
Great Architect of the Universe,’’ might advan-
tageously be made interchangeable witli “the
Great Architect of the Civil Service.” It is the
same blend of Joss.
# # #
A N.S.W. civil servant —a 400-pounder—
complained to a friend that the numerous “mathe-
matical calculations” occurring in his daily official
duties frequently floored him. “As how?”
“Here’s a question now —‘If a man gives £430
for a piece of land, how much should he give for
half of it ?’ How is a fellah, who isn’t a mathe-
matical fellah, don’tcherknow, to work out that
sort of a problem ?”
• • *
A Yic. civil servant turned up at his
office on the first of the Xmas holidays, and com-
plained bitterly at not being allowed to go to
work. This is not so incredible as it may appear;
last week’s enforced idleness jarred many a Melb.
business man whose temper and pocket alike
revolt at the idea of compulsory holiday-making.
# * #
Most bachelors in N.S.W. Parliament are
opposed to the Women’s Rights crusade, and can
work themselves into vehement pugnacity on the
subject. Most married men are willing to give the
system a try.

Several Sydney churches now regularly
give pledges enjoining abstinence from special
drinks only. The whisky-lover is pledged against
whisky, but lias all the vast resources of wine and
beer at his call; in a similar way, the beer-swiller
is pledged against that liquor, but has access to
every kind of drink that he doesn’t care for.

A well-known Southern lawyer received
instructions to issue a writ against a debtor at the
expiration of three days—the term of
notice. Before the time expired, the
debtor paid the claim to the creditor,
and as no process had been issued,
rightly declined to pay “costs.” The
creditor telephoned the result to the
lawyer, who next day billed him
thusly : “To attending you at tele-
phone re , 10s. 6d.”
An almost incredible fact.

Dear Bulletin, —In view of the recent
disclosures bearing upon Mrs. Mellon’s method of
raising the spirits of the departed, the Rev. Geo.

* * *
Dear Bulletin,— My sympathy writhes
at the news that Mrs. Mellon, the spook-trainer,
is “ dwindling away.” I am the more horrified,
as my heart went out towards her directly I read
that she once remarked, “You know how skinny I
am,” or words to that effect. The admission per-
plexed me, for all the lady-mediums 1 have seen,
from Mrs. Hardinge-Britten to Mrs. Evans (and I
have seen a few), have been “fine,” taking,
forceful, rounded women—magnetic to the male
eye which loves its pound of flesh. It is the
contour that hypnotises bold, bad man. Who
would give a fig to see a skeleton in a heaving fit ?
Not I, i’faith. Plumpness in distress, juiciness in
the wobbling throes of going-offness, massive
beauty in dishevelled perturbation, six-inch-deep
firmness evolving a hollow voice and an epileptic
madness of vision—this, ah ! this is the real
spookiness with its hair down ! You feel it.
You breathe it. It is not contagious, nor
infectious, but it is worth your money.
You can believe anything with the miraculous
vision before you. This is bang-up science. But,
of course, it has its irritating counterblast. Every
male “ meejum ” that I have had the honor of
believing to be a fraud has been a skinny, angular
man, a brassy, flashy individual sometimes, or a
silken-voiced reptile that smelt snaky all the time.
Uobbiys, when 1 patronised him, had red hair !
Most of the male spook-finders revel in black.
But they all run greatly to hair —like professional
mesmerists. Evans (the slate-writer) had a cast
in his eye. Is my experience the experience
generally of others? — The Dipsomaniac.

Miss Ethel Pilcher, daughter, of Mr. C. E.
Pilcher, the eminent Sydney Q.C., was married
at St. Stephen’s, South Kensington, to Mr.
William Cunliffe Pickersgill Jay, son of Mr. Carl
Jay, of 34 Q.men’s Gate Gardens, London, and
Blendon Hall, Bexley, on 14th Nov. The bride,
who lacks the intense gang froid of her father, was
excessively nervous, and spoke so low that people
asked if she had fainted; but she looked charm-
ingly pretty in white satin trimmed with lace,
and the usual accessories. There were four brides-
maids—Miss Skarratt, Miss Muriel Pilcher, Miss
Fraser and Miss ,1 iy —who wore remarkably ugly
dressos of so ue brown stuff, trimmed with beaver,
white waist bands and red throat bands. Brown
hats trimmed with beaver and brims lined with
red ribbon formed the head-gear. They carried
foliage bouquets tied with bright red ribbon. The
church was crowded, many Australians being
present, of course. R“cepti >n at 147 Queen’s
Gate, where so many Sydney people stay that it
has come to be known as a branch of Botany Bay.
* * #
Apropos of certain Xmas meals given to
tne poor of charitable institutions, how it must
harrow the souls of the noble-minded and generous
people chiefly instrumental in providing these
leasts to find their names remorselessly blazoned
forth in the public prints! “The meat was all
provided by Mr. and Mrs. So-and-50,”—50 joints
to (say) the Lepers’ Home, 90 joints to the Dorcas
Corroboree Society, and so on. To see thus
advertised the good deeds done privately, and
out of sheer benevolence, is enough to make
the modest and unselfish people, who thus endea-
vour to brighten the Xmas of the afflicted, die of
sheer squirm. The Bulletin sympathises from
the bottom of its liver with the affronted philan-
thropists, and trusts that on future occasions they
will be successful in their endeavours to evade the
vulgarity of publicity.

A Melb. solicitor called at a house recently
to collect some costs that were due. When he got
back to his office he said to his partner : “ I can’t
go there again. It cost me 10 s. , and I got no
costs. The family were starving.” It must be a
bad case that moves a lawyer not only to forego
his costs, but also to put his hand into his pocket.
• • •
The melancholy suicide in England of Mr.
Lloyd Burdekin, medical student, eldest son of
Mr. and Mrs. Sydney Burdekin, has caused wide-
spread sympathy in Sydney, where Mr. Burdekin,
sen., is known as the best and most generous of
Australian landlords—he has never enforced a
distress-warrant or “raised the rent ” on an old
tenant—and Mrs. Burdekin as one of the most
charming of Australia’s daughters. The afflicted
parents can only take consolation in the fact that
their unfortunate son’s death, though it _ came
about by his own hand, was just as much in the
course of nature as if lie had died from fever : for
the letters he left behind show clearly—despite
the old and unscientific law still maintained by
the State, in defiance of all the teachings of
nlienistic experience—that a man may know the
inference between right and wrong and still be
utterly powerless to do what is right.

Could not a monthly hospital subscription,
say of half-a-crown, be taken from healthy young
fellows under 25, entitling them to admission
when ill ? It would pay and prevent the horrors
of being laid up in a lodging-house. What hospital
will lead ?

In Australia, when a fireman receives per-
manent injury wdiilst performing his perilous
duty, there is no fund to pension him. A tin
tablet and a seat at the street corner are probably
his lot. The more dangerous your occupation, the
more paltry the pay.

Mrs. Charming’s dress surprises her
acquaintances. She moves in the best circles, and,
although not extravagant, is tastefully and fashion-
ably costumed. Mr. C. has been “rocky’ for the
last four years, and finds a difficulty in procuring
his cigars. The solution of the mystery is that
Mrs. C.’s relatives are rich squatters, and her
drapers find it pays them to overlook her modest
(for she has a conscience) account of about <£so per
year, in consideration of the fact that she always
accompanies her relatives on their buying-excur-
sions. As their bills reach some -£l2OO per
annum, the fifty is only a modest commission
after all.

* * *
In artist Peel’s pupils’ exhibition (Melb.)
for tills year (a most creditable all-round show, by
the way) there is a river sunset picture, decidedly
happy in coloring and freshness. It is the
work of a lady, 84 years of age, who, until
the last twelve months, had never handled a
brush. Besides this phenomenal old lady, there
are two other “juvenile” lady pupils who can
both count 70 summers, and who have also done
excellent work.
« • •
The average Marine Board resembles
nothing so much as a reversal of the temperance
chart illustrating the pulse of a moderate drinker.
Just after a big wreck it is so oppressively vir-
tuous and prudish that for a captain to misplace a
fender when pulling into a wharf means an 18
months’ suspension tor a certainty. Three weeks
later the same mistake probably calls for a fine,
and after two months the heinous crime dwindles
to the proportions of a plate broken by the China*
man in the galley.
# * *
Dear Bulletin,— The Australian Institute
of Journalists in Melbourne, were it only game,
would subject the literary civil servant to some-
thing lingering and humorous with boiling oil in
it. It is the man who holds a comparatively fat
and comfortable billet in “the service” who looks
up local editors with offers to do this or that
special work —sporting, etc.—mainly for honor
and glory; payment no consideration, don’t you
know. As payment is a very great consideration
with most proprietors, he usually gets the work,
and is satisfied with a little cash, and whatever
kudos is derivable from his position as So-and-so
of the Blunderer, or the Weekly Wail, and the
poor reporter turns bootblack or goes village-
settling. When the democracy sees the equity of
one-man-one-billet, newspapers will not enjoy the
advantages of this State-subsidy.—S.S.

Mayor Tucker, who beat the National
Ass at last Adelaide municipal elections,
is a six-foot native of that city, and is 38
years old. For three years be was Chief
Magistrate of Port Adelaide, and he served
one year as alderman of the metropolis
before climbing into the civic chair. He
has Parliamentary ambitions, and next
election will run for a country constituency
in the Kingston interest.

A man has been fined in Melbourne
for running a popgun gallery in the
Eastern Market, and allowing his patrons
to form pools. At the same time, at the
big Catholic Church bazaar at the Exhibi-
tion, the fathers of the Church were
scooping in half-crowns by the hatful with
unblushing mechanical gambles, for run-
ning which a layman would have been
skull-dragged off any racecourse in the
country. Holy Church was short of means,
and the means justify anything under such
circumstances.

The most popular charitable Melb.
institution is that of the Little Sisters of
the Poor, Northcote, a magnificent build-
ing, erected by the godliness, gumption
and go of 19 little women. Though not
French, they all speak with a French
accent, through being trained in France.
They take care of 216 old men and women,
irrespective of creed.

The New Woman’s war-cry : Liberty,
Equality, and No Maternity,
The discovery made by Sydney Univer-
sity authorities that someone personated a student
at a recent exam, comes none too soon. Reports
that such practices are common have been current
during the examinations of both Melbourne and
Sydney Universities for years past. It is sugges-
tive that if the parties to the conspiracy now
discovered had not quarrelled and exposed one
another the University authorities would know
nothing of the scholastic deception.

The official genius of Vic. Public Works
Dept, shines brightly in connection with the
Melb. G.P.O. sparrow-question. The birds have
been plentiful this year, and to keep them from
defacing the buildfcig it was proposed to enclose
the walls with wire-netting. The latest idea,
however, is an official recommendation to breed
mutton-birds in the clock-tower, which birds, it
is hoped, will swoop down on and destroy the
sparrows!

MAYOR TUCKER, of Adelaide.
It is rumoured that another attempt will
be made, now that a new Mayor of Sydney has
taken office, to induce him to order the removal of
that splendid old oak tree on Phillip-st. pavement.
Ex-Mayor Manning was urged on several occasions
by certain muddled official intellects to order its
destruction ; but the hot water into which he got
over the demolition of the Bent-st. figs made him
fight shy of the job. The tree must be at least
80 years old, and would have been cut down long
since had John Starkey, the owner of the con-
tiguous property, not viewed it with something
like affection.

Fine descriptive writing from the Age ;
Once seated in the mayoral chair, Mrs. Yates began to
ride the high horse, and naturally enough the councillors
of Onehunga refused to allow her to walk over them with
hobnailed boots on.
Mrs. Yates is smarter than we thought. The
middle-aged lady who can sit in a mayoral chair
on a high horse, and at the same time walk over
councillors with hobnailed boots is too versatile to
be in a council. She should be in a circus, at
the very least.

The Parson’s prayer. He’d not be bored
With troubles that the poor afford—
“ Give peace within our time, 0 Lord !”
The Fat Man’s prayer. He piles his hoard
By sweating Hunger’s empty board—
“ Give famine in our time, O Lord !”
The Worker’s prayer. His withered gourd,
A Heav’n as future, far reward—
“ Give Sheol in our time, O Lord !”
What doth His pitying love accord
This chorus of petitions poured ?
We know but what we see, O Lord !
Henry Lawson: An Australian Poet.
Henry Lawson’s friends will be interested to hear
that his first volume of collected work is pub-
lished. It is a poor little, badly-printed volume,
and contains only a dozen or so examples of the
writer’s skill in verse and prose (and those not all
of his best); but every Australian should invest a
shilling in a copy—firstly, because the book is
well worth it; secondly, because it is a charac-
teristically Australian book, one of the few really
original attempts towards an Australian litera-
ture; and, thirdly, because the author will be
thereby encouraged to produce a bigger volume in
better form—a form more worthy of his genius.
For Henry Lawson has a real streak of genius
perhaps, as poet and story-teller united, a
more luminous streak than any other man
in Australasia. The Bulletin knows plenty
of clever writers, but it dues not know another
than Lawson who could write the sketch called
“The Drover’s Wife,” which takes up twelve
pages in the badly-printed little book. You can
ransack the whole realm of Australian prose with-
out finding a mate for that sketch, brim-full of
the humor and pathos of the bush.
# * *
Henry Archibald Lawson was born on the Wed-
din Mountain diggings, near Grenfell, N.S.W., on
17 June, 1867. His father was Peter Hertzberg
Larsen, a Norwegian; his mother, Louisa Albury,a
nativeof Guntawattg, near Mud gee, N.S.W. Thus
Lawson’s name is really Larsen ; bub the Angli-
cised form appears to have been nearly always
used by his fattier, who is “ Peter Larsen ” in his
marriage certificate, but Peter Archibald Law-
son ”in Henry’s birth certificate. -I he Archi-
bald ” is an unlucky and somewhat humorous
misunderstanding on the part of the minister who
officiated at the christening, and who, being rather
deaf, mistook “ Hertzberg ” for “Archibald, and
sent the young Australian author through the
world with an English instead of a Norse middle-
name, much to his father’s disgust.
» • •
| JHenry Lawson’s father was the son of a Norsk
teacher of navigation, and a clever man with
brain and hand. His mother, Mrs. Louisa
Lawson, is still living, and is well-known in
Sydney as a speaker and writer, being proprietress
of the woman’s paper, Dawn. Henry Lawson
much resembles his mother, from whom he in-
herits many of bis mental traits. His maternal
grandfather is a fine specimen of the old Kentish
yeoman, a “powerful uncultured man,” and bis
mother has been “in love with poetry” since
childhood. It cannot be doubted that her dreams
and aspirations have largely influenced her eldest
son.
* * *
At the time of young Lawson’s birth his father
was a miner, but when be was a few months old his
parents took a farm at Wilbertree, near Mudgee,
N.S.W. His father gradually drifted into con-
tracting, and in helping him and assisting on the
farm, with occasional trips round the country,
Henry acquired the beginnings of his intimate
knowledge of life “out back.” When be was
17, he came with bis mother to Sydney,
where he learnt the trade of a coach-painter. His
first published composition was “The Song of the
Republic,” contributed to The Bulletin in 1888;
and since then his pen has never been idle.
years ago, on tlio death of his Henry and
his brother, a promising young musician, went to
Westralia, and later he was for a short time on
the staff of the defunct Queensland Boomerang.
He has also travelled in Victoria and Maoriland,
following his trade as a painter and writing verses.
« * #
In person Henry Lawson is slight, but tall and
muscular, with prominent features and large,
eloquent brown eyes. He suffers from deafness,
the result of an attack of fever in boyhood. Of
the merit of his writings there can be no question.
Henry Lawson is the voice of the bush, and the
bush is the heart of Australia. He has its mirth
and melancholy, and commands at will laughter
and tears. He is a keen observer; he has a fine
imagination; he is a born poet, dowered with the
poet’s passion and sympathy. He lacks culture
and critical insight, his sense of form is crude; but
these things may come to him, for he is young-
only 27 —and his work grows steadily better as he
matures. Yet Art can never give him a tithe of
the charm which he holds in direct fee-simple from
Nature.

The Xmas number of Young Australia is
particularly bright and cheery. Louis Becke and
Ernest Faveno lead a corps of interesting story-
tellers ; the number is liberally illustrated ; and
art and literature are of a quality rarely provided
for a children’s newspaper. The publisher, Miss
Fotheringhame, deserves much credit for her
enterprise.
Dr. H. L. Ellis, the well-known surgeon,,
late of the honorary staff of Sydney Hospital, has
gone to Coolgardie with the intention of practising
his profession. The diggers will find in Dr. Ellis
a man and a brother. Airs. Ellis stays behind in
the East for the present, but later on Coolgardie-
will be brightened by the advent of one of the-
most charming and popular of Sydney women.
“ Nuggets in the Devil’s Punchbowl,” by
Andrew Robertson (sent to The Bulletin by-
Angus and Robertson), is a collection of common-
place stories, written round about alleged Austra-
lian shearers who quit the shed without asking for
their cheques and are guided by a dream to a gold-
mine illuminated by the words of the 91st Psalm.
There are also a bushranger, a snake-bite, and a-
“lost in the bush’’—together with one possibly
unconscious joke, which describes the police who
may be spotted anywhere by their rig as “a uni-
form failure.” There is nothing immoral about,
the book, anyway.
Editor Bulletin, —These incidents in the
life of the Rev. C. F. Garnsey, of Sydney, lately
deceased, will at least show that some parsons try
to practise what they preach. About the year ’73
the most virulent form of scarlet fever became
epidemic in Windsor (N.S. W.). Of one family
six members were attacked, and no one would go>
near the house save the doctor and Mr. Garnsey,
their parish clergyman, who at once installed him-
self as helper. Four died within three days, amt
the good parson performed the last offices for tins
dead in each case. The two who recovered —the*
present writer is one—owe their lives to his care.
Late one afternoon a boy, son of a police constable-
in Windsor (not of Mr. Garnsey’s creed)-,,
was drowned while bathing at the junction of the-
South Creek with the Hawkesbury. Mr. Garnsey
heard of this on returning home about 11 p. in. and
ascertained that the body had not been recovered.
At once he set out alone for the spot and about
midnight stripped and dived in to try and find d.
After some time he struck against it, lying at Un-
bottom of the river. Mr. Garnsey’s strongest point
w as his influence with men. He had seen many
sides of life. He had done hard, manual work ;
had managed a sheep-run in Tasmania wit.,
assigned servants under him ; he was a fine ridei,
and at one time a good cricketer. All men worthy
of the name who met him recognised in him tl.n
true grit.—A. J.

Mv Dear Moorabinda—
I saw the serpentining Earl of Yarmouth as he
was being towed round Sydney the other day.
Without his blonde curls and ruddy rouge,
Viscount Heel and Toe somewhat resembles a
ripe peach—he is so pink and downy-looking. No
wonder he th'ougbt of framing himself in a girl’s
wig, for his is the kind of visage that looks lonely
without a butterfly bang. But it would really
need a very Sherlock Holines to spot him for a
terpsichorean. His flat - fopted, seven - league
British stride betrays none of the thistle-down
lightness that should belong even to the every-
day amble of premiere danseuse.
I have not yet seen the distinguished visitor in
his favorit - rdle of teetotum and his wonderful
lace-petticoats. When I did view him he was
simply attired in a baggy suit of tweeds with a
pale-blue necktie : also a crutch-handle stick and
a silver cigarette case as large as a slice off a tin-
loaf.
A Melbourne matron perpetrated the horrible
mistake of is-uing pasteboards for a dance in the
fag-end of November, “to meet the Viscount.”
And when the short, pink E trl refused to be joss
on the occasion, and declined the invitation, the
lady calmly “postponed” the flare-up—presum-
ably until the Earl should change his mind.
An Australian belle who married into the
nobility has had, so goes the tale, a parlous bad
time with her relafcions-iu-law. Instead of the
marble halls in which her friends framed her in
their dreams, her domicile was a tumble-down
little shanty far from the Moated Grange, and the
contumelious treatment she received at the hands
of her husband’s relatives was quite up to Cin-
derella stan lards. Now, however, the air has
cleared. Thugs have happened—that Australian
girl’s turn has come, and woe to her patrician
connections!
A, ropos of snei d give-and-take, a most charac-
e istic story is going the rounds re an Australian
e .-Premier, whose legal pansy-blossom is, for
•-(line unknown reason, not on sisterly terms with
certain vice regalities. When some frisky society
maidens imbibed not wisely but too well at Guv-
mentouse, the Governor of the day fled next
morning in great disorder to recount the whole
adventure to the politician who, as a patriarch of
the province, might be relied upon to know the
ropes. “Tell me, is this sort of thing usual at
Government House?” gasped the horrified poten-
tate. “Ho yes, Hi believe so,” at once answered
the statesman in his inimitably languid style,
“and that’s just why Hi never let Lady X go.”
“ By the way, I want to speak to you on that very
subject,” began the Governor, lamely, but here
the Great Opportunist drew himself up in all his
majesty. “Not one word, your Hexellency,”
said he, in a commanding squeak, “Lady Hex
could nevah be hinduced to visit hat Government
’Ouse.” It was indeed a noble instance of presence
of mind.
There was no V.R. patronage for the last Sydney
Liedertafel concert of the season. In Melbourne,
Gov. ’Opetoun has a deep-rooted objection to the
little band of dress-suits who upend themselves in
the Town Hall to warble about the flow’rets
closing at eve, and so on. Lord H. only patron-
ises them in his hair-shirt moments, so to
speak. Indeed, His Victorian Ex. openly declares
(more openly than ever, now that his barque is on
the seafthat if he had any real power he would
issue a ukase making persistent Liedertafel singing
an indictable offence.
Fisk’s Jubilee Minstrels, under the guidance of
organ-voiced Mr. Orpheus M‘Adoo, made a cheer-
ful re appearance at Sydney Centenary on Boxing
Night, and had to sing a double programme
owing to the pressure of encores. Encoring isn’t
a feminine vice, and women don’t applaud with
their feet. I have only known one woman (I am,
of course, speaking in a strictly dress-circle sense)
to do so, and the vigor with which she stamped
after every item provoked much astonishment.
“I am glad to see,” said someone, “that you are
not too English to show your pleasure.” “Oh,
that’s not the reason,” replied she, with her two
knees going like churn-sticks. “My feet are so
wet, yon know, and I must keep the blood circu-
lating !”
But the M'Adoo minstrels warble with so much
natural fervor that it certainly is difficult to
refrain from provoking them into doing it all over
again. Time, apparently, has no effect on the
robust tenor of Miss Mattie Allen —the rare bird
of the show—or on M’Adoo’s soothing and pro-
found bass, which he apparently produces from
his boots. Then there is a lady who would make
a barrel of tar look pale, and who possesses a
dramatic soprano voice of bell-like quality ; with
a quadroon mezzo-soprano and an upright he-
tenor, who all help to make the jubilee choruses
the attraction they undoubtedly are. It was a
clever idea, too, I thought, for the ladies to all
wear black gowns, which gave their —er —sunburnt
complexions every chance.
I am in receipt of the “ Grace ” waltz, by Sydney
Turnbull, of Bananaland, who has dedicated his
by no means commonplace little effort to Lady
Norman, altho’ Her Northern Ex. isn’t exactly
the sort of lady one would accuse of a leaning to
the light fantastic toe. But ambitious compos ra
never stop to consider the fitness of things. An
Australian M.P., whose gout was (and is, I ex-
pect) simply horrid, once had a hopping schottishe
dedicated to him by a thoughtless lady friend, and
I shan’t be a bit astonished when somebody
inscribes a rowdy comic song to the honor and
glory of Primate Smith or Cardinal Moran.
“ The Grace ” waltz is a prettily tuneful,
smooth and eminently danceable measure, and
quite deserves the triumphal sale it is alleged to
nave achieved in Brisbane. From Mr. Percy
Abbott comes a copy of “ My Lad,” a sailor song
without a shipwrecked corpse, which is alone
sufficient to ensure its popularity. Further
advantages are a waltz-refrain and a photo, of
Mr. Abbott (singer and composer) on the cover.
“ My Lad ” is catchy and ought to be popular—if
only because it isn’t dedicated to a duke. By the
way, I wish the publishers of these bursts of
heavenly harmony would put a little more starch
in the paper they print them on. None of the
home-grown sheet-music can stand on its hind
legs on the music-rest.
One of the things that took my fancy in “ The
House that Jack Built’’—the pantomime at Her
Majesty’s—was the fact (made evident by the
attire of Dame Trot) that women aren’t accumu-
lating many new vanities as they stagger down
the ages. I call it a distinctly encouraging thing
when the comic she-man has to reach over to the
tie-back skirt period in order to satirise feminine
fashions, and the serio-comique is forced to allude
to the long-abjured bustle. It looks as if the latter-
day female doesn’t intend to look as much of a
fool as she used to —though Fred Dark (Mother
Hubbard) in the second act, as an “Advanced
Woman ” in full tartan knickers and a butterfly
bonnet, pretty well pillories all the feminine
eccentricities that threaten to keep male creation
on the broad grin before very long.
Miss Florence Esdaile (late of the Royal Comic
Opera Co.) as the Princess, wears two breezy
toilets, and that's not counting the cabbage-green
satin, criss-crossed with black, worn in the first
act, that blends so charmingly with “Jack’s”
pale heliotrope suiting. In net 2 she wears a
Nautch drapery of pale pink strips of satin with a
plethora of barbaric ornaments, but she commits
the mistake of wearing corsets, which spoils the
true inwardness of the thrilling pink singlet made
familiar to ns by Nellie Stewart. The end and
aim of the Nautch costume is to suggest that the
lady (in a state of nature) has jus . thrown a few
sequins at herself and trusted to them sticking
where they were most needed, so the bare sus-
picion of whalebone spoils the picture, as it were.
Miss Esdaile’s wedding-toilet, of white and silver,
was an elegant fancy dress, especially when
backed up by the fetching Flo. Forde, as “Jack,”
in pale moonstone-colored satin, with long cloak
of wattle-color and three yellow plurne,s in her
knave-shaped hat. Miss Forde’s long heliotrope
tights are also visions of loveliness, but I didn’t
admire her get-up of carubia red and yellow—it
was too reminiscent of fat beef.
They are not exactly over-dressed in “Jack.”
Take that fetching cocoa-coloured “ boy,” with the
statuesque limbs, who stands on the o.p. side, par
exemple. She is simply and radiantly attired in
a suit of brown fleshings cap-a-pie, with a yard or
so of gaily-striped satin judiciously draped and
trickily finished off with a few golden spangles
which add tons to the attractiveness of a singularly
fetching costume. Perhaps the most artistic item
in a rather gorgeous show was when the witches,
transformed into rose-pink fays, seasonably attired
in one layer of chiffon, danced a measure among
flames of fire. It had a most weird look, and 1
only hope none of the infants gazing with goggle-
eyed astonishment will strive to emulate the flame-
effects in the kitchen when the authority has her
back turned.

bers’) was the young millionaire, heir to the huge

and at present living en prince at a big Sydney
hotel. He is said to choose his chums on the most
radical principles, and to find more joy hunting
bugs and butterflies than in p wading toe haunts
of fashionable belledom. The gilded traveller is
a tall and strapping over-six-foot blonde.
Buf I haven’t finished talking about the Summer
Cup. A lady of a legal family, in a stylish white

of stiff white linen with an edge of embroidery -

pressed that get-up to his manly bosom would feel
as if he were hugging Joan of Arc with her fight-
ing clothes on.
Apropos of cricket, the sad end of Mr. Lloyd
Burdekin, eldest son of Mr. Sydney Burdekin,
who shot himself in England recently, is now said
to have been due to the defeat of the Australians
in the recent test cricket-match, England versus
Australia. It is said that the unfortunate young
fellow laid plunging - odds" on the Australians
winning the game, and the news of their defeat
was probably too much for his misguided sense of
patriotism, even if not for his pocket.
Sydney Royal was simply crowded out on
Saturday night, when the ever-popular Bland
Holt continued his wild career (begun on Boxing
Night) with a drama of the most Blandholtian
dye, yclept “The Span of Life.” Though
Walter Baker (who appeared to sport three gold
brooches in his black cummerbund) was the con-
ventional hero, the whole audience seemed to
consider that Mr. Holt, as the versatile “Nutty,”
was the principal male character, and cheered
accordingly. Pretty Hilda Spong played the rdle
of heroine in an angelic sort of white gown, with
long fichu of frilly wh'te chiffon; a Nancy Lee
kind of white dress with blue collars ; and a brown
garment and widow’s weeds, in which Miss S,
looked so fetching that a hero of any artistic
discernment would never have dreamt of turning
up alive again and spoiling so eminently becoming
a costume.
Lively Mrs. Bland Holt, who is never so wel-
come as in a strong comedy part, never missed a
point in the Malapropish dialogue of the servant-
girl ShroveTuesday, and waswarrnlyreceivedby the
gallery—the tribunal whereby most professionals
stand or fall. Shrove isn’t as dressy as a milliner’s
dummy, but dressing isn’t the strong point of the
“ Span of Life,” which relies on a chain of hair-
curling sensations to keep people clamoring for the
right to hang by their eyebrows in the auditorium.
What I like about Mr. Holt’s scrupulously-staged
productions (any number of horses and a camel
figure in “ The Span of Life ”) is that they play
with such dead earnestness that they always look
as probabl ■as the Ten Commandments. And I
think the villain in “ The Span of Life ” (played
by Mr. Cosgrove) is the most realistic villain I
have ever met—knowingly, at least.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Dear Bulletin, —The £5OO exemption-
clause in Vic. Land-Tax Bill was simply a des-
perate Tory trick, accomplished with the aid of
bogus Liberals in the teeth of honest Radical
opposition. The exemption was intended to give
the Legislative Council a ghost of an excuse for
rejecting, the Bill, and its immediate resultr,
clearly foreseen, was Sargood’s retirement from
the Ministry. Wrote Sargood to Premier Turner :
“I strongly deprecated this alteration, as you will
remember, seeing that, while it relieved 160,000
persons from a very small tax, it threw this extra
burden upon the shoulders of a comparative few.’’
There was the softgoods-importbr’s excuse for
going over boldly to the enemy. You suggest in
your article that the aforesaid 160,000 small land-
owners might have kicked against the tax. It was
not until the large men had abandoned all hope of
sympathy from the little men that they decided
to incite the cry of “unfair legislation ” by voting
for the £5OO clause. The extremest Tories in
the House subsequently had the hardihood to
attempt an increase of the exemption to £750.
That gave the game away.—Yours, Enquirer.
What is termed by the daily papers the
“Outrage at Coombing Park station” has been
the principal excitement of the past week. Ac-
cording to the story, Bertie Pulsford, son of the
great N.S.W. Foreign-trade panjandrum, was a
night-watchman at Coombing Park, when the
premises took fire, and two men—assumed to be
the usual wicked unionists —were seen running
away. Then Mr. Pulsford gallantly rushed after
them and fired at them, whereupon one of the
pair “turned round, aimed ‘deliberately ’ at the
gallant watchman with a revolver, and shot him
through the thigh.” This story rests chiefly upon
Pulsford’s own evidence, and, in the meantime,
the police have failed to find the two men, and
even suggest the theory that Pulsford wasn’t
“deliberately” shot at by anyone, but that his
own revolver went off in his pocket, and that he
is laboring under a mistake. The S.M. Herald,
in an inconsequent sort of fashion, adds the
following rather confused narrative to the present
story ;
This is not the first occasion on which Mr. Bertie Puls-
ford has shown pluck and determination in dealing with
d mgerous men. Some time ago, at the same place, Mr.
Bertie Pulsford faced a gang of 50 men who attempted to
foree their way into a shed in which free laborers were
sleeping. With a companion he charged the company
and rode right through them. He then heard a shot fired
some distance away, and, leaving his comrades to guard
the door of the shed, when in the direction of the report,
he ran as fast as he could, and suddenly found, iff the
dark, that a man had levelled a gun at him. Presenting
his revolver, he called upon the man to stand, and the
welcome and unexpected reply showed that it was Mr.
Withers, the manager of the station, with whom in the
darkness he h id hv a timely call escaped coming into an
unfortunate conflict.
According to this narrative Mr. Pulsford, on a pre-
vious occasion, rode right through 50 “ dangerous”
men who, to all appearance, made no attempt to
interfere with him. and then scooted into the dim
distance to attack his own colleague who was
firing promiscuously in the background. The
fact that the only man he seems to have attacked
on this occasion was his own boss suggests that
he is a somewhat hurried young man liable
for making mistakes when he rushes around in
the dark, and, therefore, it might be as well to
suspend judgment in the present instance until
further evidence comes to hand. A flurried youth
who is tearing around at night on a plain often
doesn’t really know who or what him, or
whether he was chasing a unionist or a station
r on.
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Melbourne’s evening paper tried hard to boom
Howell’s bowling in the Vic.—N.S. W. match. One among
several pars., concerning the new man, announced that —
“ Howell’s performance against the Victorians, thus far,
reads 2 wickets for 26 runs 1” Which was not at all
remarkable, considering that 9 wickets for 130 had fallen
at time of writing. When the bowling averages for both
innings of Victoria came to be made up Howell was
fourth on the list. Turner and Callaway did as much, or
more, execution at considerably less expense, while
Newell’s average for two wickets was fractionally better
than “ the demon's."
* * * •
An odour of gate-money pervaded the inter
provincial match at Melbourne. Play didn’t start until
2 o’clock on the Saturday and Monday, in order that
there might be plenty of match left to attract a big
Boxing Day crowd. The policy was a comparative failure.
Long ere closing-time on the first day 9 Victorians were
out for 130, and when the last wicket was found to be
giving a lot of trouble the public had an excuse for
sniffing at such an odd circumstance. A game that com-
mences with the avowed purpose of carrying its interest
up to a given period is not a genuine sporting affair,
anyhow. Cricket-watchers said so by stopping away
from the M.C.O. ground in considerable numbers when
Boxing Day arrived.

out including Lyons or George Giffen.

One noted bowler, when “on the job,’’ is a
study in flesh. In running, the lower part of his dia-
phragm wobbles. To render himself bendable without
danger to himself or spectators, he coaxes aside with his
left hand the masskcontainiug his centre of gravity, and
picks up the ball with the right.

Dear Bulletin, —In last week’s paper you state
that young Trumper, the N.S.W. boy-bat, who has been
playing such excellent cricket of late, is a pupil teacher at
Crown-street (Sydney) Public-school. In this you are
wrong. He is still a pupil and is captain of the school
team, having for his vice-captain Laurie Deane, brother of
the well-known players, Messrs. Syd. and Arthur Deane,
and himself a player of no mean repute. It is a coincidence
that Noble, who scored 152 (not out) against the English-
men, formerly occupied the position of captain of the
same school team. The school possesses an excellent
record, not having been beaten in school matches for 10
years.—Alpha.

* * * *
Each of the English pro.’s took away a lump
of the Sydney putty wicket which so largely contributed
to their first international win. They are sending ’Ome
bits of it to be placed under glass cases in the various
county cricket pavilions.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin, —I saw the ladies’cricket-match
at Bathurst, on Boxing Day, and it really wasn’t half
bad. The fielding was the weakest feature, for the dear
girls wouldn’t back up. The batting was capital, the
Misses Gregory rattling up over a hundred between them
in no time. I expected underhand bowling, and was
agreeably surprised to find it the exception. A Miss
Keene took the bowling honors with a capital delivery,
moderately fast and with a decided break!!! What do
you think of that? But the dressing (ordinary length of
skirt) was all wrong, and until it is improved on ladies’
cricket won’t gee. The skirt, if the wind is kind io
the batswoman, blows in front of the wicket and mak s
an effectual shield. One lady was given out leg before
wicket, and I trembled for the umpire. To sum up, no
girl should play after she is 20 at the most, for when that
age is reached they don’t like sacrificing dignity for
speed. Tne younger girls all through the match were the
best players, always, of course, excepting the Misses
Gregory, who are “to the manner born.”—Yours, C.E.H.
* * * *
Victorians are not too well satisfied with their
representatives in the second test-match. Blackham,
who represents the province on the selection committee,
is captain of the Melb. team, and it is singular that of the
three men picked from Victoria two are always from the
Melb. team. This time Bruce, H. Trott, and Trumble are
picked, and Bruce and Trumble are Melb. men. Neither
of them has exhibited form this season, and yet they go in
instead of men like Worrall, Harry, or A. E. Trott.
Worrall, who made 96 against N.S.W. on a bad wicket, L
a splendid field and a good change bowler, whilst both
Harry and A. E. Trott have shown well this year.
« * * ♦
The board behind the wicket nearest the
entrance-gates of Sydney Cricket-ground is located some
feet within the play, and much interferes with the move-
ments of the nearest fieldsman. Ward was several times
baulked by it, and one catch he would assuredly have
held had it not been for the thing necessitating his stop’
ping when running at full speed.

That good sportsman and generous host, “Ted ”
Underwood, of Quirindi station, N.S.W., was killed by
falling from a horse on Xmas morning—to the great grit f
of his many friends.
