Carbine is to be sold in March. Geo. M'lvor
has compiled a pedigree plan (to be printed) of the breed-
ing of Carbine, wherein are included the names of no less
than 2000 horses. The plan is like that published when
Nordenfeldt was in the sale-ring ; the number then tabu-
lated was 1500.
» » * *
Whispered that Carbine’s future home will pro
bably be in France.
• ♦ ♦ *
Blarneystone’s Summer Cup win was greatly
due to the judgment displayed by jockey Tierney. Mar-
vel’s full brother had a year and a pound the advantage
of the Trier, who ran a good race, but there were a couple
in the ruck who might have been more prominent had
their people so ordained. The winner was backed at from
12 to 20 to 1.
* * * *
All the races were productive of good finishes,
except the Steeple, won by the Joker. There were
n.ime.oiis accidents. The Ace carried 251 b. overweight,
a.id a lik' dat the palings, where two others came down.
Tin- A.I.C. was lately petitioned to remove this dangerous
obstacle, but refused.

J. D. Lewis, owner of Tarcoola, has bought
Bruin (who ran second in Caulfield and fourth in Melb.
Cup) with the intention of taking him to ’Frisco. Judging
from the success of second-rate Australian horses in
America, Bruin (whose best distance is six furlongs) ought
to more than pay his way, as the majority of the big
Yankee prizes are given in sprint races.

W. R. Wilson see'us to hold a remarkably
strong hand of two-yea< olds. First Challenger, then
Wallace, and now Balance. The best of the three seems
to have a bit of a mortgage on next year’s Derby.

The gallant old Albeit «oems nearly done at
last. In Saturday’s chapter ot accidents at Randwick
he faced all obstacles and got second money, but
after a mile he could hardly raise a gallop. He was ridden
by “ Banjo,” a poetic obituary of whom has already been
written by a brother bard. In the first two lines “ wreck”
rhvmes with “ neck.”
» « ♦ •
Dear Bulletin, — As it is impossible to repress
gambling, why not regulate it by making bookmaking a
misdemeanour, having totalisators worked by Govt, officials
at every race-meeting (only), the Govt, to take 5 per
cent, and the club 5 per cent, of the investments, and let
the Government run sweeps d la Tattersall on the big
events during the year? This would reduce the evil to a
minimum and get rid of the bookies and cronk jockeys.
The clubs could then give stakes sufficient to induce every
owner to be a goer. It is disgusting to see lazy fellows
standing in Irus abutting the principal streets taking
shillings and had-crowns from office and errand-boys in
the name of sport.—Yours, &0., Z.
* * * *
The demised N. R. D. 15 >nd, sec. of the Vie.
torian Amateur Racing Club, devised the numbered
saddle-cloth. It is generally adopted in America, and is
spreading in Eng land.
An eccentric figure lately disappeared from the
English turf in “ Mr. Manton"—the Duchess of Montrose,
ajtat 76. Despite her age she comported herself as if 40
years younger. Scorning the fashions, she invariably
turned up at races in a flaming red dress, and her horses,
which were most successful, carried a fac-simile ot it.
During the four years prior to her recent retirement from
active racing, she won about £62,000 in stakes alone. Her
second husband, Slirling-Crawford, who died in ’B3, raced
horses in her ‘‘all scarlet” jacket, and won many rich
classic events. The Duchess thought more of Fred.
Archer and other leading jockeys than she did of anyone
else, and it was thought she would marry a knight of the
saddle. She didn’t, choosing, at the age of 70, Mr. 24-year-
old Henry Milner—brother, by the way, of the present
S.A. Governor’s private secretary. Their marriage caused
a sensation in English society, and so did their separation.
♦ * * *
In Suva, Fiji, the elite “sports,” when hard np
for something to wager over, organise dog-fighis. All
dogs left alive (generally bull-terriers) are in the final
“ go."
* * * *
On a recent Sunday morning a race for £5O a-
side, from Dunnville (W.A.) to Coolgardie, 38 miles, came
off between L. Levinson’s pony Beauty and Gregory’s
mare Dolly. The horses were started from opjesite
the Wealth of Nations Mine, at 6.30 a.m. Levinson, on
his pony, reached the finish, Leaver’s Hotel, Coolgardie,
at 8.32 a.m., thus doing the whole distance in 2h. 2m.
Beauty is 13J hands high and carried 9st. 41b., being
only bush-fed. The road was hard, stony, rough and
sandy. Doily was about an hour behind.
» * * *
At Brighton Beach (Melb.), on Boxing Day,
happened a raw-meat prize-fight. At 11.30 the principals
stood in the ring, stripped and accompanied by the
customary bottle-holders, &c. There were about 709
people present, including most of the local bookies,
whose vehicles made a close encircling rampart. Twenty-
nine earnest rounds were fought in a littlji over an hour,
and at the finish both men, whose identities are hidden
under the names “Bill” and “ Ned,” W’ere considerably,
though not irreparably damaged. Meantime, the police
were engaged in shooing lovers out of the scrub.
* ♦ * *
At Hannan’s Rush, Westralia, a boxer was
beaten through swallowing kerosene which his second and
bottle-holder had brought in mistake for something more
reviving. Had he trained in the cheap bars of Sydney, as
many pugs do, he’d never have noticed the difference.
* * * •
At Ellerslie (M.L.) three horses ran a dead heat.
A Chinaman rushed to the tote-office, flourished three
tickets, and demanded his div. “Me all li’. Me gotee
the ticket, Wha’ foh no payee ? Lun lacee again ?”

“ Victor ” writes : —Georgie confirmed the good
opinion I have here expressed about her by winning the
Deo. Stakes from Bob Ray and Arihi, albeit the latter
made a grand fight of it under her 101 b. penalty, and but
for being blocked just above the distance would have at
least very nearly won. Bob Ray greatly improved on his
Melbourne form”, and will win a good race for Mr. O’Brien
this Autumn. Sir George will improve with age, and will,
as a three-year old, do credit to his sire. The Australian
Peer.
Moorefield has developed into a splendid horse,
and will yet prove himself a champion over any distance
up to a mile.
Loyalty’s ignoble displays in the A.J.O. Handi-
cap and Summer Cup were not calculated to inspire his
Tatt. Cup backers with confidence, yet I venture to
predict that if not to-day (Saturday) yet before the end
of the season we shall see him run a far different horse.
In trusting to Newman instead of The Captain
in the Summer Cup, Mr. Kelso threw away any chance he
had of landing that race. Newman has certainly proved
a most expensive horse. The Captain should, however,
be returned a winner ere many weeks are over.
In the Summer Cup, Donation certainly ran the
best of my original selections. His recent gallops have
done him much good, and he may bid boldly for Tatt’s
Cup. For the actual winner of this race, however, com-
mend me to my long-selected Tridentate, followed by
Warminster, Loyalty, and Donation.
At the past A.J.O. meeting there was amongst
the majority of the jockeys (?) riding a more than usual
display of the barbarity of mercilessly flogging their
mounts when hopelessly beaten. It really is time for the
stewards to take action in this matter. In many cases
owners might well make their lads ride whipless; for
race after race is thrown away by boys who flog their
mounts out of stride instead of riding them home vigor-
ously with hands and heels.
Recent magisterial decisions seem to interpret
the law that the man who acts as an agent for a tote
proprietor, and bets in the street, is not a principal, and,
as a result, the “ portable tote ” is now an accepted
Adelaide institution, and is the new employment for all
persons out of work. In Adelaide, if two sparrows settle
on a telegraph line, a tote-man immediately arrives and
accepts investments, paying out on the sparrow that
flies first.

“ Never saw anything like the fish in those
Tasmanian rivers. It was fling your line and pull out
your fish—such whoppers ! —all night long. Four of us
one night fished six hours and basketed 1137. And when
we had finished we noticed that the river’s level had
fallen 2ft. 2£in. Fact, I assure you."
The success of the new Cannalin remedies
for piles is most remarkable, large quantities
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-street, Sydney.
Sydney Wool Sales.
M'Burnby, Wallis & Co. reportNo sales have been
held during the week including Christmas holidays, as
buyers claim the right of a rest after the incessant
valuing they have been compelled to cope with since
opening of the wcol season. On 3rd Jan. we will submit
a catalogue specially got up to include small clips. In
this manner small lots will command full attention of
buyers, who cannot be distracted by the presence of
larger clips as they may sometimes be in a mixed cata-
logue. Judging by private transactions prices are
equal to closing rates of last auctions. On 10th Jan. we
will bring forward a catalogue of about 2000 bales of
large clips from New England, Bathurst, Monaro, Goul-
burn, and other districts from which wool is now arriving
in the city.’ Sydney, 31st Dec., 1894.

Eureka stockade flag is said to be in the
possession of Mr. J arnes King, Minyip View (Vic.),
whose father, it is averred, took it down from the
flagstaff on the stockade after the fight. Some
small strips were torn from it, but with that ex-
ception and some bullet-holes it is in good order.
To meet an insistent demand that this
characteristic Australian book should be procur-
able in more permanent form, “A Golden
Shanty,” by Bulletin writers, is now supplied in
neat cloth binding at 25., post free to any address.
It contains some of the best and most original
work which has helped to make The Bulletin
famous, and no Australian library is complete
without it.
The British Society of Psychical Research
recently sent circulars to a vast number of persons
asking if they had ever seen a ghost or other
supernatural manifestation, and requesting par-
ticulars of the apparition if one had been seen.
Out of 17,000 who responded, 655 men and 1029
women claimed to have either seen a spectre or to
have had reason to believe that one was about.
This marked preponderance of the female element
suggests that a good many she-ghosts are really
hysterics. And if she-ghosts are largely hysterics
then there ensues the further possibility that he-
ghosts are sometimes whisky.
A young Englishman (Wittingham) was
lately condemned at Paris to 20 years’ imprison-
ment for the murder of his French wife. The
proceedings at the trial read curiously unlike those
of British courts. General Wittingham, the
prisoner’s father, was openly blamed by the Pro-
curator of the Republic for his absence of due
affection. “ You have failed in all your duties as
a father,” urged the official. “ You may have
been a brave soldier and a gentleman ; you might
in your pride of caste have at first repulsed the
poor girl who was your son’s wife; but, as her
conduct was irreproachable, you should have
received her who was to become the mother of
your grandson.”
William Saunders, of Rue Australia, to
his friend Watson, of Dubbo (N.S.W.): “I reckon
myself the worst sort of fool. This place does not
suit a single man at all and few married ones.
There are over a hundred gone from here, and
they are scattered over the country, and some of
them are working for Is. Bd. per day, and have to
find themselves out of that. For any clothing
you buy, you have to pay as much as you do in
Australia. The only things that are cheap are
tobacco and rum. You can buy rum for about
Bd. per bottle, and tobacco for 2d. per lb. The
natives of the country are very homely people.
The women all smoke and do all the work, and
the men simply do the looking on. There are
very few men in the country. They nearly all got
killed in the war. The women are not what you
might call pretty. They are mostly copper-
colored. They never wear any boots, but they
are very clean and good dancers. ... I am
working the blacksmith’s shop, but the summer
will be too hot to work at any trade. . . Wild
beasts are not so plentiful as represented. . , .
If I could get enough money to carry me to a sea-
port, I would soon be in Australia. I would
sooner be in gaol there than free here. . . It is
all paper money here. The Paraguayan dollar is
only worth about 7d. of English money. The
morals of the country are none to boast of. There
are very few marriages, and of the children born,
70 per cent, are illegitimate.”
A Sydney evening paper publishes edito-
rially a statement that Govt, surveyors’ work is
plotted on the map without check-survey. The
Bulletin is informed that the district surveyors
make a check-survey twice annually. Whatever
the shortcomings of the surveys were in the old
days, when they were performed, as often as not,
by means of a rum-bottle and the table of a
big landed proprietor, the system in operation in
N.S.W. is as little open to criticism as the
Ordnance survey of Great Britain—perhaps the
one department of English administration to
which no suspicion of jobbery ever attached.

'J'HE Index, an International Review of Insurance, Trade and Finance, writes as follows respecting the
LIVERPOOL & LONDON & GLOBE
Total Assets — INSURANCE COMPANY. -£9,083,790
For years past sensationalism has been rampant in the fire insurance world; and to find'that a Company which
has steadily declined to d viate fr in the quiet far-seeing and self-respecting policy which has given the Liverpool and
London and Globe its proud position, which has refused to be tempted into any lurid display of financial fireworks,
continues to so easily outdistance all its competitors, is a special pleasure and gratification to those who wish to pre-
vent fire insurance from degem rating into a mad scramble for other people’s loaves and fishes, and who desire to see
the dignity of the profession worthily upheld.
The flie department for the past year shows an increase of premium income of over £43,000, and a trading account
surplus of £70,954, or about 4J per cent, of the premiums. The figures for three years will serve by way of com-
parison :
Premium Surplus on
Year. income. Losses. trading account.
1891 .... 1,479,783 .... 989,605 .... 29,309
1892 .... 1,585,195 1,029,567 .... 79,513
1893 .... 1,628,224 .... 1,070,585 .... 70,954
This Company has a wise and straightforward plan of keeping a fire re-insurance fund, altogether apart from its
general reserve, and we should like to see this adopted by every fire office. Each year, as the premium income in-
creases, a portion of the trading surplus is added to this fund, maintaining the amount at about 45 per cent, of the
premium income. The amount so transferred on the present occasion is £36,000, bringing up the fire re-insurance
fund to £740,000, and leaving £44,954 to be carried to profit and loss account. By this method not onlyds the un-
expired liability constantly and separately provided for, but the true profit of the year is honestly shown. The
adoption of this system would not at all suit many companies ; but in this, as in other respects, the report of the
Liverpool and London and Globe is a model one. Against a premium income of £1,628,224, the total fire funds of the
Company, exclusive of the paid-up capital of £245,640, and allowing for dividend now stand as follows:
General Reserve .... ... £1,300,000
Fire Re-insurance Fund ... .... 740,000
Balance of Profit and Loss Account.. . .... £61,392
£2,601,392
This is more than one and a-half times the total premium income, a magnificent financial position to occupy.
Offices : PITT & SPRING STREETS, SYDNEY.

OTICE OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
In the matter of the application of The Perpetual-Trustee Company (Limited), mortgagees under
memorandum of mortgage from Thomas Bartlett, No. 143,218, dated October 31, 1888, over the
whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 768, folio 139.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as

that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until February 8, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under
him, unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy

interest up to the time of such sale.

When Gladstone dies, the enormous
English hypocrisy will look in vain for a smug utter-
ance to equal his. Reports of Armenian atrocities
have stimulated the exploiter of Bulgarian horrors
to say [vide cable) that as a reward for the Turks’
cruelty and injustice God has reduced their ter-
ritory by half during the last generation. This
nauseous cant comes well from a representative of
the nation which annually massacres its tens of
thousands of victims to social injustice, aristo-
cratic tyranny, and capitalistic greed. Throat-
cutting is a merciful death compared with that by
starvation in London slums, or by disease in the
Midland factory-hells.
Dear Bulletin, —The use of firearms to
frighten, and not to kill, is becoming ordinary in
N.S. Wales. The papers reported two cases on
two consecutive days. In one case, a station-hand
fired over the head of a man who was running
away from him (so goes the story), and the latter
returned the shot, with effect. Then a Sydney
publican, whose house was being bombarded with
blue-metal, came out to remonstrate with a shot-
gun, and “fired in the air,” lodging a good pro-
portion of the charge in a youth’s back. The
police called this “self-defence.” A man is only
legally justified in using a deadly weapon in self-
defence when he has just reason to think his own
life in danger. He must not use such a weapon
in endeavoring to arrest even a felon or murderer,
if the arrest can be effected in any other way.
That is the law, let the police say what they
will.—R.
“Maoriland,” for which The Bulletin
has consistently “ barracked ” for years, has found
its way into London Athkilbum. This gives an
imprimatur to the term. Why doesn’t Premier
Seddon abolish “New Zealand?” It will be
done sooner or later, and he might as well have
the credit of the change.
Russia hasn't a pound of coal on the Paci-
fic, and every ton of fuel used by the men-of-war at
Yladivostock has to be imported from Oronstadt.
Lately a Russian ironclad on its way to join the
Pacific squadron ran out of coal and put into
Capetown. The Cape dealers asked .£8 a ton and
the Russian had to pay. In war-time he could
have got none at all.

OF WORKS
-FXHIBITION
Of the
PHOTOGRAPHIC ART,
To be Held in the
MELBOURNE EXHIBITION GALLERIES
From 29th JANUARY till 30th APRIL, 1895.
The Exhibition Trustees have determined to hold an
Exhibition of the Photographic Art in the Galleries con-
nected with the Aquarium, and cordially invite the
assistance and co-operation of all those who are interested
in the Photographic Art.
Th® Exhibition will embrace all the various branches of
the art, and will include Portraiture, Landscape, and
Scenery subjects, together with specimens of Photo-
gravure, Autotype, and all the various processes of repro-
duction of pictures from photosrraphs, together with an
exposition of the application of Photography to Art
Industry, and Commerce. F J *
A special section will be placed at the disposal of
amateurs for the exhibition of their work, and a separate
room will be placed at the disposal of the Amateur Photo-
graphic Association of Victoria.
The Exhibition will be open on 29th January, and
remain open till April 30, 1895. All exhibits must be
printed W ‘ th ex P lanator Y label, legibly written or
The trustees reserve the right to reject any proffered
exhibits, and to remove any objectionable labels &o if
necessary. * ’
Each exhibitor of photographs, &o„ of not Isas than
£lO in value will be furnished with a pass for entry during
the currency of the exhibition. 3 “
All exhibits must be delivered at the Exhibition-build-
ing not later than January 19, and placed as directed by
the trustees. 1
Entry forms and all further particulars can be obtained
on application to the undersigned.
J. E. SHERRARD.
Secretary Exhibition Trustees.

is continued up to the year 1794. As in the case
of Vol. I, the materials from which the work has
been prepared comprise the letters and despatches
of the principal officers in charge at Sydney, and

many of which have never before seen the light.

Ten of Us.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Ten of us, eager as men may be,
Rode through the night to the distant sea.
One of us, riding with reins held slack
Stumbled and fell on the stone-strewn track;
And one of us turned by his side to stay,
But eight of us carelessly galloped away.
Eight of us, eager as men may be,
Rode through the night to the distant sea.
Where the track led over the black-soil loam
One of us slackened and turned for home;
And one of us rested his galloping beast,
To join in the dance of a bridal feast:
But six of us, eager as men may be,
Rode through the night to the distant sea.
Lured by the lights and the cheer within,
Two of us paused at a wayside inn ;
And three of us stayed at the edge of a wood,
Where a bevy of beckoning women stood :
Leaving of all the ten, but me,
Riding alone to the distant sea.
Lm isbane.
K.

Dear Bulletin, —Looking over the balance-
sheet of the Australasian Institute of Journalists,
I find that the largest sum spent to Sept. 30 was
£l7 for badges—ridiculous little trinkets, the proud
members’ signboards ; and about the smallest sum
disbursed was .£3, which extravagant amount was
squandered upon destitute journalists. Up to
date nothing but empty grovel has been bestowed
upon the Creat Moguls of the society, David Syme
and Sam Winter; but when the outstanding subs.,
.£206, are collected these gentlemen will probably
receive a silver statuette apiece.—Yours, S.

Dear Bulletin, —Rev. Wm. Brown,
Methodist Missionary to New B'itain, writing to
the Australian Methodist Missionary Review
r marks, in reference to returned Kanakas from Q.
plantations :
They go away from their homes simple savages, beguiled
by the sight of the trader’s merchandise, and with the
prospect of some “ trade ” to call their own at the end of
their three years nf hard labor in the sugar or cotton
plantations ; but when they return they come with their
mouths full of “ pidgin ” English and filthy conversation.
1 know all this is true because I have talked with some of
these returned laborers, and have been grieved to think
that it was from white men that they acquired this un-
clean conversation full of the vilest oaths. And there in
that village were men who had serv d their three years in
the white man’s land, and yeWwere as keen to taste of the
human flesh as the savage pure and simple.
With regard to which one would like to ask a
few questions, viz.—(L) Are there, no missionaries
whose duty it is to look after the souls of the
niggers on the plantations? (2). If not, why not?
Is it because a missionary in Queensland, under
the cold eyes of the planters, would have a poor
time of it compared to what he would have as a
little tin god in the island homes of the heathen?
(3). If there are such missionaries in Queensland
how do they account for the shocking general
result of their labors mentioned by the Rev.
Brown, one of their own cloth ? (4). With refer-
ence to the avidity with which, according to the
latter, they return to their human flesh-pots, can
any of your readers (who may have made a study
of the obscure subject of cannibalism) throw any
light upon the real facts in connection with the
weird statements and dark hints attributed to
European travellers in cannibal countries who
have been compelled to eat human flesh, and to
castaway sailors who have had to do so through
necessity, to the effect that the man who once eats
man oraVes for him ever afterwards? An answer
to the la-t question will satisfy my curiosity.
The others 1 don’t really care about.— Creeve
Roe.


A Lady’s Letter.
Dear Maude, Tf Tf >
It is quite impossible to say how
much I appreciate your beautiful gift.
Owing to remissness on the part of our
housekeeper, I did not receive it until
last evening, or you would sooner have
had my most hearty thanks.
The truth is, we were all talking
the other day about Tea, and wondering
where we could get really good Tea,
but hitherto our efforts have not been
quite successful. When we got the
beautiful caddy of "My Favorite" Tea
we all rushed into the kitchen, and
whilst one boiled the kettle the others
got cups and saucers, and we soon
tasted the delicious mixture. Why, it
is a Tea to dream of ! It is, my dear
Maude, simply perfect, and you are a
darling for sending us the pretty caddy.
Again thanking you for your kind
remembrance, and for having solved such
a problem in our domestic affairs.
Believe me. Dear Maude,
Sincerely yours,

SOMETHING ABOUT TWO BOYS.
" Thk most pathetic incident of my childhood is
this ; My mother had been very ill for several
weeks, and the doctor solemnly announced that
she oould not live more than two or three days
longer at most. That night my father roused me
from sleep and took me out of my little bed to bid
her a last good-bye. I shall never forget the
scene, which was new and awful to me. People
were weeping all round the room, the air of which
was heavy with the odour of caudles and lamps,
and reeking with the fumes of drugs. My mother
knew and kissed me, and then they took me back
to my bed. But ere I was led away some one
opened the window a few inches from the top, and
I noticed the grey dawn resting on the glass, and
heard the ‘cheep, cheep,’ of a newly-awakened
bird. Since then I have associated that hour and
sound with that unhappy episode.
“ But {and to say what now follows I have
written the foregoing paragraph) we were all
rasped and tortured for nothing. My mother
proceeded to get well hand-over-hand, and died
quietly thirty years afterwards. She survived
every person who stood at her bedside that night,
except me."
Speaking of the illness of her son, a boy of
nine, a lady says : “We had to sit with him
night and day, giving him brandy, wine, beef-
tea, &0., to keep him alive, and expected every
day would be his last. The physician plainly told
us that nothing more oould be done to save him.”
Yet, in spite of the disease, and —we almost
said —in spite of the doctors, the lad is well to-day.
And this is how it all came about. There is a
moral in it, too, but suppose we serve that up at
the end of the story. All right, you say. Very
well, then.
It seems that this boy, George Westmoreland,
had previously been a strong, healthy little chap,
as all boys ought to be. But about the middle of
last November —1891, that is—he was taken
down. The family couldn’t make out what ai.ed
him. He complained of a bad pain in the stomach,
and vomited a quantity of yellowish-green stuff.
Presently the pain was so sharp he couldn’t lie in
bed, and they had, so his mother says, to apply
fresh hot poultices one after another. The whites
of his eyes turned yellow, and his skin, too. He was
hot, and feverish, and had to fight for his breath.
Of course his mother sent for a doctor, and the
doctor said his young patient was suffering from
inflammation of the bowels. He gave medicines
which, however, did no good, so far as the boy’s
friends could see. On the contrary, he grew
worse, and a second doctor was fetched. This
medical gentleman differed from his predecessor,
and gave out that George had an attack of rheu-
matic fever—in other words, acute rheumatism
a disease which no boy has any business with
whatever.
The treatment on this theory availed nothing ;
George was worse. He now had a hacking cough,
and his expectoration was so offensive that the
people had to use disinfectants. He broke out
into sweats, so heavy as to saturate the pillows.
He could take no nourishment save a little milk
and lime water. He wore away to a skeleton, did
the poor boy. He was nothing but skin and bone,
and they had to lift him in and out of bed. Then
he fell so ill he would not notice anyone in the
room, and lay for hours never opening his eyes.
Then came the time when a third doctor said he
couldn’t possibly live.
What happened after that the boy’s mother
tells. We give you her exact words : “In
February last,” she says, “my husband, as a last
resource, determined to try Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup. After a few doses the boy’s breathing
was easier and he took food. In three days he
was able to sit up, and in a week’s time he was up
and dressed. He gained flesh and strength every
day, and is now able to go about. Sometimes I
look at him and can hardly believe he is the same
boy who was so recently at death’s door. Seigel’s
Syrup saved his life. Yours truly, (signed) Mrs.
Mary Westmoreland, 5, High Street, Plumstead,
London, April 27, 1892.”
Now, half a dozen words. Little George had
no bowel inflammation, nor a single touch of rheu-
matism. That was the doctors’ professional guess-
work. He had a sharp attack of biliousness and
indigestion, of which Mother Seigel would have
cured him long before had her medicine been
appealed to. Here is the moral to conclude with :
Learn what the true remedy for illness is, and use
it first instead of last.
In Japan children are taught to write with
both hands. The distinct advantage of this is
shown in the forgery returns.
One beneficial result of the Melb. Herald-
Standard amalgamation has been the temporary
abolition of boy-reporting labor in the southern
city.
The Vincentian Fathers are crusading
round Melb. suburbs with a R.O. teetotal mission
greatly to the disgust of Murphy and Hogan, wh,
keep pubs.
The Vic. Parliamentary Standing Com-
mittee on Railways recommends the construction
of a railway from Jurnburrna to Outtrim, on two
notable saving conditions —(1) that all the land
required be handed over as a free gift; and (2)
that the interested coal companies guarantee a
revenue equal to 4 per cent, on the cost of con-
struction. At last, a worthy lead from the
Cabbage-Garden!
It has at last been amply demonstrated
that a really rousing, effective funeral sermon
is productive of good results. A Christchurch
(M.L.) family who believed that two young
men relatives went down with the Wairarapa,
ordered an exceedingly sad and impressive
discourse to be delivered in the church, and two
days afterwards the boys turned up and wanted to
know if dinner was ready.
The Sydney papers have reprinted the full
text of the ponderous Times article pointing out
how desirable it was that, after being such asses
as to take their disputes to the other end of the
world for settlement, Australians should now send
a Judge in pursuit of the cases to settle them.
More approving articles have appeared in the
nobbled organs of legal opinion, without a ghost
of a hint that the matter could be settled much
more simply by abolishing appeals to England,
and letting the same Judge settle the same cases
in Australia. The job is nearly ripe for Parlia-.
mentary exposure now.
Melb. Age has a complaint to make about
the alleged Australian fleet, crying that it never
risks itself in the morass called Hobson’s Bay, but
stays always in the safe waters of Port Jackson.
The obvious necessity for warships to stay in a
safe place doesn’t strike the Aok. The warship is
the most expensive theory afloat, and even naval
experts know it isn’t safe to let one out after dark,
lest it should run into itself or fall overboard.
U.S.A. warship Atlantis lately tried its ram on a
rotten old derelict, but the derelict continued to
float, and the warship crawled in a crippled state
into port. When the Carnperdown accidentally
rammed the Victoria, the Victoria went down,
but the Carnperdown nearly went down, too, A
big ironclad is about as brittle as glass. The pro-
babilities of the first naval battle are that all the
ships will disable themselves within 15 minutes of
commencing action. Then the naval experts will
build more fleets capable of still more incapacity.
About this time democracy will rise in two or
three places at once, and will take the naval
experts by the scruff of the neck, also the Fat
Men, and princes, and Rule Britannia, and the
whole bag of tricks, and will throw them into the
sea, where they will sink down softly to join the
fleets. But the Australian squadron will continue
its picnic in Sydney Harbor—except in November,
when it will go to Melbourne Cup, if the sea is
calm.
The wheeze concerning the “ disappearing
Australian seaman ” is about the most empty
agitation that has occurred for a number of years.
As a matter of fact, there is no disappearing Aus-
tralian seamen. There never were enough Aus-
tralian seamen to make their decrease noticeable.
The most unobservant traveller can verify this for
himself, as ten out of every dozen sailors, at any
time during the memory of most people now
living, were either known as “Olsen,” “Oscar,”
or “ Carl,” and they are only too willing to accept
wages that would make the disappearance of any
Australian seaman, let alone his family, a matter
of very brief time. Thirty years ago the dis-
appearing Australian seaman was mostly English
or Scotch ; now he is mostlv Scandinavian, and
that is all the difference. Were the sailor-man’s
remuneration less of a pittance and more of a
wage, and his life not quite so much that of a dog,
there might have been an Australian seaman
whose decadence would have been a fit subject for
lamentation. As it happens, there never was one.

“The Green Carnation."
“The Green Carnation ” is a new book which has
succeeded to the vogue of “ Tlie Yellow Aster,”
and which is many times wittier and worthier to
hold the fort of notoriety. Here are some petals;—
• * *
“Goodness is merely another name for cowar-
dice. We all have a certain disease of tendencies
that inclines us to certain things labelled sins. If
we check our tendencies, we drive the disease
inwards ; but if we sin we throw it off. Sup-
pressed measles are far more dangerous than
measles that come out.”
# # •
To the suggestion of morning opera it was
objected that patrons would be unfitted for the
duties of the day. “Quite so,” said Mr.
Amarinth, with extreme gentleness. “ That would
be the object of the performance, to unfit one for
the duties of the day. How beautiful 1 What a
Slorious sight it would be to see a great audience
ocking out into the orange-colored sunshine,
each unit of which was thoroughly unfitted for
any duties whatsoever. It makes me perpetually
sorrowful in London to meet with people doing
their duty. I find them everywhere. It is im-
possible to escape from them. A sense of duty is
like some horrible disease. It destroys the tissues
of the mind, as certain complaints destroy the
tissues of the body. The catechism has a great
deal to answer for.”
» • •
“Women always expect something. Women
are like minors, they live upon their expecta-
tions.”
* # #
“ Probably we suffer from the activity of our
forefathers. When I feel fatigued I always think
that my grandfather must have been what is
called an excellent walker.”
# * *
“My wife began by trying to influence me, she
has ended by trying not to be influenced by me.
She is a good woman, Reggie, and wears large
hats. Why do good women invariably wear large
hats ? To show they have large hearts ?”
# # #
“I hate offering payment to anybody,” said
Reggie, “ even when I have the money. There is
something so sordid about it. To give is beautiful.
I said so to my tailor yesterday. He answered,
‘I differ from you, sir, in toto.' How horrible
this spread of education is !”
* # #
“Nothing on earth is so absolutely middle-class
as Nature. She always reminds me of Clement
Scott’s articles in the Daily Telegraph.”
# * *
“ A virtue is like a city set upon a hill, it can-
not be hid. We can conceal our vices if we care
to, for a time at least. We can take our beautiful
Eurple sin like a candle and hide it under a
ushel ; but a virtue will out. Virtuous people
always have odd noses, or holy mouths, or a reli-
gious walk. Nothing in the world is so painful as
to see a good man masquerading in the company
of sinners. He may drink and blaspheme, he may
robe himself in scarlet, and dance the can-can,
but he is always virtuous. The mind of the
moulin rouge is not his. Wickedness does not sit
easily upon him. It looks like a coat that has
been paid for.”
• * *
“ Respectable elderly women do so much harm.
Prolonged purity wrinkles the mind as much as
prolonged impurity wrinkles the face. Nature
forces us to choose whether we will spoil our faces
with our sins or our minds with our virtues.”
# # #
“He looks astonishingly young.” “Yes, he says
that his sins keep him fresh. A sinner with a
young lamb’s heart among the full-grown flocks of
saints, you know.”
* # *
“ I believe that money is gradually becoming
extinct, like the dodo. It is vanishing off the face
of the earth. Soon we shall have people writing
to the papers to say that money has been seen at
Richmond, or the man who always announces the
premature advent of the cuckoo to his neighbor-
hood will communicate the fact that one spring
day he heard two capitalists singing in a wood
near Esher. One hears now that money is tight—
a most vulgar condition to be in, by the way ; one
will hear in the future that money is not. Then
we shall barter, offer glass beads for a lunch, or
sell our virtue for a good dinner.”
* * •
“ To sin beautifully is one of the most com-
plicated of the arts. There are hardly six people
in a century who can master it. The artist in sin-
ning improves upon the sins that Nature has put,
as it‘Were, ready to his hand. He idealises, he
invents, he develops. No trouble is too great for
him to take, no day is too long for him to work in.
The still and black-robed night hours find him
toiling to perfect his sin ; the weary white dawn,
looking into his weary white face through the
shimmering window-panes, is greeted by a smile
that leaps from sleepless eyes. The passion of
the Creator is upon him. The man who invents
a new sin is greater than the man who invents a
new religion. No Mrs. Humphrey Ward can
snatch his glory from him. Religions are the
Aunt Sallies that men provide for elderly female
venturists to throw missiles at and to demolish.
What sin that has ever been invented has ever
been demolished ? There are always new human
beings springing into life to commit it, and to
find pleasure in it. Some day I will write a
gospel of strange sins, and I will persuade (me
P.C.K. Society to publish it in dull, misty
scarlet powdered with golden devils.”
* * *
“ Good people love hearing about sin. Haven’t
you noticed that although the sinner takes no
sort of interest in the saint, the saint has always
an uneasy curiosity about the doings of the
sinner ?”
# * *
“ People who mean well always do badly. They
are like the ladies who wear clothes that don’t fit
them in order to show their piety. Good inten-
tions are invariably ungrammatical.”
• * •
“ The moon is the religion of the night.”
* # •
“Esmi is the bravest man I know. I think
sometimes that he sins even more perfectly than I
do. He is so varied. And he escapes those
absurd things consequences. His sin always finds
him out. He is never at home to it by any
chance.”
* * *
“Good morning,” he said softly. “Is not this
marmalade Godlike? This marvellous, clear
amber glow, amber with a touch of red in it,
almost makes me believe in an after life. Surely,
surely marmalade can never die.”
# • •
“We are so frightfully punctual that I feel
quite like an early Christian. I wonder why the
Christians were always so early before we were
born. They are generally very late now.”
# # •
“I know,” said Reggie Hastings, holding his
fair head slightly on one side, and crumbling his
bread with a soft white hand. “ I know. That
is why I laughed at my brother’s funeral. My
grief expressed itself in that way. People were
shocked, of course, but when are _ they not
shocked ? There is nothing so touching as the
inappropriate. I thought my laughter was very
beautiful. Anybody can cry. That was what I
felt. I forced my grief.”

never makes a joke unless by a very bad accident
indeed.
Canterbury (M.L.) Times on the “colo-
nial ’ :
I don’t know what opinion Max O’Rell formed of
colonial manners. I have not had time to read his book
yet. But if I was a visitor from “ furrin parts”! think
I should sum up the colonial as to his manners as being
well, a casual cuss. The native-born article—who has
passed the fourth standard—is about as casual as you
make ’em. He is not ungrateful, but to express his
gratitude would compromise his independence. He
doesn’t mean to be rude, but to be polite he thinks would
savour of servility. So he is just—casual. You are
having billy-tea on top of the Fort Hills, for example, and
he comes along—sweating, puffing, generally done up.
You kindly hail him. “Would you like a cup of tea?”
Of course he would-the very thing. His stolid face
almost looks grateful : but it soon recovers its stolidity
and he answers, “ Don’t mind if 1 do.” He always says
that. Offer him a drink, a fill of baccy, a feed, or a shake-
down ; the answer is always the same, “Don’t mind if I
do.” The phrase is really a stroke of genius. In some
subtle way it shifts all the obligations on to your
shoulders, and you feel quite ashamed to think you are
not more grateful to him for accepting your drink.
Which is mostly true. Bub then Max O’Rell was
not an observer in any sense of the term, and the
casualness of the Australian “ cuss ” didn t strike
him.
A ghostly comforter writes as follows :
Dear Bulletin, — I object to the unbelieving manner in
which you refer to an existence other than this. Tell me
how it is impossible and why .it is wonderful? I was not, I
am, why cannot I always be? I desire to know the
following things {inter alia)'. (1.) Is there a supreme
controlling power? If so, are its intentions to me bene-
volent or otherwise ? (2.) Is there a Heaven ? If so, will
I get into it ? (3.) Is there a Hell ? If so, will I keep out
of it? (I.) Are you aware that, as you are reading this
essence of wi-dom, you are travelling through space at
the rate of 56 miles an hour, fastened to a rounded mass
that, viewed far enough off, reflects a pale, hardly-\ isible
light, and looks a tiny speck amidst a mass of others?
(5) Do you ever reflect that you will be dead in about ten
years, and the hands that now hold this paper, the lips
that now smile with cynical criticism will be motionless
and rigid? That your bodv will be stuffed some day in a
woodeTi box and lowered with indifferent hands into the
earth, and left there to rot? Try to contemplate your
own funeral. Does it make you feel choked and lonely ?
(6) Did you ever notice how interesting a trousers button
becomes viewed in the light of a century, or so, hence?
Primitive man required not a trousers button, what will
the future superior being use? (7) Did you ever realise
that a globular immensity was created. Various exis-
tences appeared on its surface, stamped their feet, and
thought themselves the centre of creation. You and I
appear and stamp, and prefer ourselves to the rest of
humanity. We will also disappear and others will come
after us who will not know us. They all, in due course,
will stamp and be convinced that the universe is incom-
plete without their particular existence. Yet they say
man is a free agent! I say—Bah I—Your Fellow Sinner,
W.P.B.
West by North, Again.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We’ve drunk our wine, we’ve kissed our girls, and
funds are sinking low,
The horses must be thinking it’s a fair thing now
to go;
Sling up the swags on “Condamine” and strap
the billies fast,
And stuff a bottle in the bags and let’s be off at
last.
What matter if the creeks are “up”—the cash,
alas ! runs down !
A very sure and certain sign we’re long enough in
town.
The nigger rides the “boko,” and you’d better
take the bay,
“ Quart Pot ” will do to carry me the stage we
go to-day.
No grass this side the Border-fence ! and all the
mulga’s dead !
The horses for a day or two will have to spiel
ahead ;
Man never yet from Queensland brought a bullock
or a hack
But lost condition on that God-abandoned Border
track.
When once we’re through “ the rabbit-proof ” —it s
certain since the rain—
There’s whips o’grass and water. So, it’s West
by North again !
There’s “ feed ” on Tyson’s country—we can
“ spell ” the mokes a week
Where Billy Stevens last year trapped his brumbies
on Bough Creek.
The Paroo may be quickly cross’d —the Eulo Com-
mon ’s bare;
And, anyhow, it isn’t wise, old man ! to dally
there.
Alack-a-day 1 far wiser men than you and I
succumb
To woman’s wiles—and potency of Queensland
wayside rum.
Then over sand and spinifex ! and on, o’er ridge
and plain !
The nags are fresh—besides, they know they’re
Westward-bound again.
The brand upon old “ Darkie’s ” thigh is that
upon the hide
Of bullocks we must muster on the Diaraantina
side.
We’ll light our camp-fires where we may, and
yarn beside their blaze ;
The jingling hobble-chains shall make a musn
through the days.
And while the tucker-bags are right, and we’ve a
stick of “ weed,”
A swagman shall be welcome to a pipe-full and a
feed.
So, fill your pipe ! and, ere we mount, we’ll drink
another nip,
“ Here’s how that West by North again may
prove a lucky trip,”
Then back again—l trust you’ll find your best
girl’s merry face,
Or, if she jilts you, may you get a better in her
place.
Warrego. The Breaker.

The Old Bark School: An Echo.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was the first Monday after the holidays. The
children had taken their seats in the Old Bark
School, and the master called out the roll as
usual ;
“ Arvie Aspinall.” . . . “’Es, sir.”
“ David Cooper.” . . , “Yes, sir.”
“ John Heegard.” . . . “Yezzer.”
“Joseph Swallow.” . . , “ Yesser.”
“James Bullock.” . . , “Present.”
“Frederick Swallow.” , . . “Y’sir.”
“James Nowlett.” . . . (Chorus of “Ab-
sent.”)
“ William Atkins.” . . . (Chorus of “ Ab-
sent.”)
“ Daniel Lyons. ” . . . “Perresent, sor-r-r. ”
Dan was a young immigrant, just out from the
,*od, and rolled his “r’s” like a cock-dove. His
brogue was rich enough to make an Irishman
laugh.
Bill was “ waggin’ it.” His own especial chum
was of the opinion that Bill was sick. The mas-
ter’s opinion didn’t coincide, so he penned a note
to William’s parents, to be delivered by the model
boy of the school.
“Bertha Lambert” . . . “Yes,’air.”
“May Carey” . . . “Pesin’, sair.”
“Rose Cooper” . . . “Yes, sir.”
“Janet Wild” . , . “Y-y-yes, s-sir.”
“Mary Wild” . . .
A solemn hush fell upon the school, and pre-
sently Janet Wild threw her arms out on the desk
before her, let her face fall on them, and sobbed
heart-brokenly. The master saw his mistake too
late; he gave his head a little, half-affirmative,
half-negative movement, in that pathetic old way
of his ; rested his head on one hand, gazed sadly
at the name, and sighed.
But the galoot of the school spoilt the pathos of
it all, for, during the awed silence which followed
the calling of the girl’s name, he suddenly
brightened up—the first time he was ever observed
to do so during school hours—and said, briskly
and cheerfully:—
“ Dead—sir !”
He hadn’t been able to answer a question cor-
rectly all the morning.
“ Children,” said the master gravely and sadly,
“children, this is the first time I ever had to put
*D ’ to the name of one of my scholars. Poor
Mary! she was one of my first pupils—came the
first morning the school was opened. Children, I
want ye to be a little quieter to-day during play-
hour, out of respect for the name of your dead
schoolmate whom it has pleased the Almighty to
take in her youth.”
“Please, sir,” asked the galoot, evidently en-
couraged by his fancied success—“please, sir,
what does ‘ D ’ stand for?”
“ Damn you for a hass ! ” snarled Jim Bullock
between his teeth, giving the galoot a vicious dig
in the side with his elbow.
HENRY LAWSON.
A Northern legislator attributes the disease
affecting pine apples in the Moreton district (Q.)
to the fact that the farmers “do not saw off the
dry limbs from the trees often enough.”
The Unspeakable Englishman.
Grant Allen’s truly awful tale, “ A Triumph of
Civilisation,” in the Graphic Christmas Number,
is the latest and most maddening injustice to
Australia. G.A. is a pretty good minor philo-
sopher, and his novels are tolerable; but as a
short-story writer he chiefly ranges from cheap to
nasty and back again. The scene of “A Triumph
of Civilisation ’’ is laid in the Gulf of Carpentaria.
The heroine is on a steam-yacht, which meanders
into the Gulf during a “thick, dismal fog,
worthy of Newfoundland banks,” and gets
wrecked on a “coral reef” with "jagged peaks ”
and “jutting pinnacles.” “ Breakers’’—in-
cluding “a curious white wall of water” —
“pound” the vessel “with incredible energy”
upon “the fierce fangs of rook.” The fog, by-
the-by, has lasted for several days, during which
the yacht’s master “ could get no observations,
the sky was so dim; and was compelled, for the
most part, to go by dead-reckoning.” Anybody
who has never been in the Gulf will recognise the
truth of this description at once. It is a devil of
a place for fogs, coral reefs and breakers.
But the story is waiting. Naked Australians,
armed with long spears (as is, of course, the
fashion in the Gulf country) swarm whooping
over the heroine—Australian natives always
whoop—and “dragher cruelly by the hair to their
encampment, prodding her from time to time
with a spear to make her go faster.” Then the
heroine disappears for 10 years. At the end of
that time a famous explorer (probably Lumholtz)
comes to lecture in London, and tells the legend
of a white woman kept in captivity among the
N.Q. blacks. The heroine’s daughter, Camilla,
is at the lecture ; and when she hears the legend,
“a thrill, cold as ice, ran down Camilla’s spine.”
She at once determines to go with her husband and
free the captive. Another steam-yacht is char-
tered, and they come out to Australia. The geo-
graphical detail is simply gorgeous :
They cruised cautiously down the East coast of the
Gulf, for even to this day, in spite of surveys and charts,
the tropical shores of Carpentaria are but little known
to European mariners. That great bight lies so far out
of the track of modern commerce that none but pro-
fessional explorers ever penetrate jar into its useless cul-
de-sac. [This is a nasty jar for the Burketown mail-
steamers.] . . . Anchoring in a small bay, under
shelter of a low headland of ruddy rock(!) just crowned
by a few gaunt screw-pines and towering blue-gum
trees (!!) they put out a boat in the direction of a sandy
beach (!!!) where a faint blue column of smoke showed
the probable site of a native encampment.
More naked blackfellows (it seems that the
whole coast of Carpentaria is lined with naked
blackfellows) rush frantically down to the water’s
edge, waving more spears. They say that the
white woman is eight days’ march westward, and
westward Camilla goes.
So for two days they crept on along a reef-bound coast,
with most intricate navigation, hugging the shore within
the rocks, where their scanty charts availed them any-
thing.
Singularly enough, they didn’t meet any
steamers; but after two days’ slow steaming they
come to another sandy beach, and there Camilla
and her husband and the doctor go ashore, and
find three children ready waiting for them—
“ranging from three to eight years old.”
Camilla raised her glasses again and took a long, good
look at them.
“ Why, they are not so very black; they’re only
brown 1” she exclaimtd in some astonishment.
The doctor looked grave os he stepped ashore, firmly
grasping his revolver. “ Well, ye—es,” he said, in a
strangely restrained voice. “They’re brown—light
brown ; a great deal lighter, in fact, than I ever saw three
native Australian children.”
A sickening sense of horror came all at once over
Camilla’s soul.
Then you sit tight and read a horrible descrip-
tion of Camilla’s mother, illustrated with a yet
more horrible picture. She is “naked, save for a
kirtle of rough grasses as worn in Carpentaria;
and she throws hersejf at Camilla’s feet, while her
dusky brood cling round her knees. Then Ruth
Mordaunt rises to the situation. Shall “those
sailors in the background spread the tale of
Camilla’s mother’s shame, to vex Camilla’s soul,
through the London newspapers?” Never-r-r !
The doctor’s revolver hung loosely in his hand. Long
habituation to the shifts of savage life had given Ruth
Mordaunt the skill and courage to act promptly in emer-
gencies. With an impetuous dart forward the wild
woman seized the deadly weapon. Then she stepped
three paces back. Camilla, appalled, clung wildly to her
husband. Ruth Mordaunt raised the weapon with un-
trembling hand. In the plenitude of her motherly love,
in the infinity of her mercy, she fired two cartridges—
one at Camilla, the other at Eric. In a second two bodies
lay reddening the white sand with their blood in front of
her. Before she could raise it again and turn it against
her own heart one of the sailors rushed forward and
wrenched the pistol from her hand. But Ruth Mordaunt
was not to be baulked of her own self-inflicted punish-
ment. Breaking away from the man’s grasp, like the
savage she had half become, she ran lightly along the
sand and sealed, with hands and feet, one of the rocky
pinnacles that jutted into the water. One moment she
paused with hands and arms extended on the topmost
peak; then she plunged into the deep basin and held her
breath below till the life was gone out of her.
The doctor turned to the crew with a face of mute
83’tnpafhy. “That was finely done!” he said, after a
long, deep pause. “No other way was possible ! ’Twaa
a triumph of civilisation I She. died, after all, like an
English lady /"
!!!!!!!!!
And this compound of grotesque bathos and gross
ignorance is solemnly put before the public, not
as a wild freak of “English humor,” but as a
serious essay in literature by a leading English
author.
The central axiom of Theosophy is “ He
that hath eyes to see let him put his head in a
bag.”—Westminster Gazette.
Regularly every Monday morning, writes
a correspondent, a cart may be seen driving
up to a certain station within 20 miles of
Sydney, and depositing on the Govt, railway-
platform a couple of casks of illicit spirits
which are duly forwarded to the city by the next
State-owned train which oomes along; and as
regularly as that cart puts in an appearance with
its illegal load of blue ruin, the local policeman
may be observed thoughtfully scratching his chin
and looking hard in the opposite direction.
Wairarapaevidence. Davis, A. 8., said:—
“It took me all my time to prevent witness, French,
getting into the boat."
French, ex-marine :
“ I wish to vindicate my honor as an Englishman and a
man. I never attempted to get into the boat. I would
not attempt it, neither in that boat nor any other boat.”
2nd officer :
“ Dr. Harris was the first I took 00 the raft into the
boat. He begged to be taken in.”
Dr. Harris :
“I was not the first to be taken into the boat. I did
not beg to be taken in. I only got in because I was
entreated to do so.”
In such oases men contradict each other in perfect
good faith. Their perception is be numbed.
The Dreamer .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A dreamer lay in a land of flowers
And scarcely stirred as the hours flew by;
For bees hummed ever amid the bowers,
And the locust chanted drowsily ;
And the dreamer dreamed of a thousand towers
With golden turrets against the sky.
What if, away in the fields of grain,
The reapers worked and the sun beat down ?
Poor worms ! they toiled while his busy brain
Had built great ships and a white-walled town—
Had been with crowds who would shout again
To deck his name with a world’s renown.
What if, away on the dusty road,
A band of mourners drew slowly nigh.
And a weary man had laid down his load
In the race of life ? As the bier passed by
The dreamer smiled, for his dream-land showed
A nation mourning when he must die.
But the air grew chill, and a last late bee
Flew over his head on its homeward way,
And a breeze came up from the far-off sea
And stirred his hair ; it was thin and grey;
And a spectral shape stole silently
Adown the lane where the dreamer lay.
The dreamer spoke : “ I must rouse me soon ;
I have worlds to win—I have much to do;
I have only slumbered an afternoon
While the day was warm and the skies wew
blue.
And a bed of grasses, with roses strewn,
Brought scented sleep as the swift hours flew.”
But Death stood waiting amid the flowers :
“ What canst thou show for thy life ? ” he said,
41 A fair dream oity with golden towers ! ”
Then touched him once on the brow and fled.
The bees were dumb in the jasmine bowers,
And the dreamer slept with the unknown dead.
Queensland.
THANE.
The second reading of the Adult Suffrage
Bill was hung up for several days in the S.A.
Assembly because % member had gone amissing,
and the most energetic search failed to bring him
to light. His vote was needed to make up the
Constitutional majority of 28. Strange stories are
told about the places which the whip ransacked in
his vain search for that lost member, and the
houris whom he met in the course of his investiga-
tion, and the language that he heard, and the
weird drinks that he encountered. Finally, the
absentee turned up, and refuted the dark sus-
picions against him by proving that he wasn’t in
any of the places where his party had been looking
for him.

•‘Evert man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different eirole and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you .can' possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived bv The Bulletin.
